Original Permissibility in Marriage: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Inter-madhhab dispute over implication of descriptive attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that marriage remains permissible despite the absence of a prohibiting attribute and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
Original permissibility in marriage is not negated by absence of a specific attribute; only clear evidence can overturn it.

This passage explores a key methodological dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools: does the absence of a described attribute (like freedom) imply the absence of the ruling? The Hanafis argue that original permissibility remains; the Shafi'is analogize to zakat. Understanding this disagreement illuminates the Hanafi principle of al-asl fi al-ashya' al-ibahah.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
The Hanafi school holds that marriage is originally permissible unless proven otherwise. Absence of a prohibiting attribute does not change this.
This is grounded in general Quranic verses like Q 4:24 and Q 4:3.
2
Identify the Shafi'i argument
Al-Shafi'i argues that if a ruling is tied to a description, its absence negates the ruling, analogous to zakat on grazing camels.
They rely on the hadith 'On grazing camels is zakat' as a paradigmatic case.
3
Analyze the Hanafi rebuttal
The Hanafis respond that not all attributes are causes; multiple causes can exist. The general verses establish permissibility broadly.
They give examples of words used for specification, emphasis, or other purposes.
4
Distinguish two meanings of condition
The Hanafis differentiate between a condition as an external prerequisite (like wudu for prayer) and a conditional attachment. Only the former negates the ruling when absent.
Only the first type entails that the ruling is negated by its absence.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practically, Hanafis permit a man to marry a free woman on top of his slave wife, while the Shafi'i view may restrict this based on the absence of the freedom attribute.
This highlights the real-world impact of the usul disagreement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فبقى الحكم على تقدير طول الحرّة على الحل الأصلى (١١) ، وكذا في الأمة الكتابية. ترجيحاً من غير مرجّح ؛ ولأن مثل هذا الكلام يدلُّ على علية هذا الوصف نحو في الإبل السائمة زكاة، فيقتضي العدم عند عدمه. وعندنا : لا يدلُّ ؛ لأن موجبات التخصيص لا تنحصر فيما ذكر نحو: الجسم، الطويل، العريض، العميق، متحيز ، وكالمدح والذم ، أو التأكيد نحو: أمس الدابر لا يعود، أو غيره نحو : في وَمَا مِن دَابَّةٍ فِي الْأَرْضِ ) (٢). فلم يوجد الجزم بأن كل الموجبات منتفية إلا نفي الحكم عما عداه ؛ ولأن أقصى درجاته أن يكون علة، وهي لا تدل على ما ذكرتم ؛ لأن الحكم يثبت بعلل شتى، ونحن نقول أيضاً بعدم الحكم، لكن بناءً على عدم العلة لا أنه علة لعدمه. انتهى وقال أيضا : «ومنه التعليق بالشرط يوجب العدم عند عدمه عند الشافعي عملاً بشرطيه، فإن الشرط ما ينتفي الحكم بانتفائه. وعندنا : الـعـدم لا يثبتُ به ، بل يبقى الحكم على العدم الأصلي ؛ لأن الشرط قد يقال لأمر خارج يتوقف عليه الشيء، ولا يترتب عليه كالوضوء، وقد يقال : للمعلق، وهو ما يترتب عليه الحكم، ولا يتوقف عليه، والشرط بالمعنى الأول يُثبت ما ذكرتُم لا بالمعنى الثاني». [١) قوله على الحل الأصلي ؛ أي الثابت بقوله له بعد بيان الحرمات : وَاحِل لكُم مَّا وَرَاءَ ذَلِكُمْ ﴾ (٥) ، وقوله : فَانكِحُوا مَا طَابَ لَكُم مِّنَ النِّسَاه ) () إلى غير ذلك من الآيات المطلقة، والحاصل أن هذه الآيات دلّت على كل امرأة إلا ما دل الدليل (1) المستفاد من قوله : فَانكِحُوا مَا طَابَ لَكُم مِّنَ النِّسَاه ) (النساء : ١٣، و وَأُحِلَّ لَكُم مَّا وراة ذلِكُمْ ﴾ [النساء : ٢٤]. (۲) الأنعام : من الآية٣٨. - (۳) انتهى من «التنقيح»، و«التوضيح»(١ : ٢٧٦ (۲۸۰) باختصار وتصرف. (٤) أي صدر الشريعة في التوضيح شرح التنقيح (۱ : ۲۸۱). (٥) النساء : من الآية ٢٤ . (٦) النساء : من الآية ٣.
Thus, the ruling remains upon the estimation of the free woman's superiority on the original permissibility, and similarly for the scriptural slave woman. This is a preponderance without a preponderant, and because such speech indicates the causality of this description, like 'on grazing camels is zakat', so it necessitates non-existence when it is absent. And according to us: it does not indicate, because the causes of specification are not limited to what was mentioned, like body, tall, wide, deep, occupying space, and like praise and blame, or emphasis like 'yesterday the passing does not return', or other like in 'And there is no creature on the earth' (6:38). So there is no certainty that all the causes are absent except the negation of the ruling from what is other than it; and because the utmost degree is that it is a cause, and it does not indicate what you mentioned, because the ruling is established by various causes, and we also say the non-existence of the ruling, but based on the non-existence of the cause, not that it is a cause for its non-existence. It ended. And he also said: 'And from it is the attachment by condition which necessitates non-existence when it is absent according to al-Shafi'i, acting upon its conditionality, for the condition is that by whose absence the ruling is negated. And according to us: non-existence is not established by it, rather the ruling remains on the original non-existence, because a condition may be said for an external matter on which something depends, and it is not entailed, like ablution, and it may be said for the conditional, which is what entails the ruling and does not depend on it, and the condition in the first sense establishes what you mentioned, not the second sense.' [1) His statement: 'on the original permissibility' means the one established by His statement after explaining the prohibitions: 'And lawful to you are whatever is beyond that' (4:24), and His statement: 'So marry what seems good to you of women' (4:3), and other absolute verses. The conclusion is that these verses indicate every woman except what evidence indicates.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafis and the Shafi'is regarding the implication of conditional or attributive language in legal texts. The text begins by stating that the ruling (permissibility of marriage) remains on the original permissibility (halal asli) when considering the superiority of a free woman over a slave woman. It then presents the Shafi'i argument that descriptions like 'grazing camels' in the zakat verse imply that the ruling (zakat) is causally linked to the description, so when the description is absent, the ruling is absent. Similarly, they argue that the absence of a prohibiting attribute in marriage implies permissibility. The Hanafi response, attributed to the author's school, is that descriptions do not necessarily indicate causation. Many other factors can cause a ruling, and the absence of one possible cause does not prove the absence of the ruling. They give examples of words that can be used for specification, emphasis, and other purposes. The text then transitions to a discussion about conditions. The Shafi'i view is that a condition necessarily negates the ruling when absent. The Hanafi response distinguishes between two meanings of 'condition': one that is an external prerequisite (like wudu for prayer) and one that is merely attached to the ruling. Only the first type entails the absence of the ruling when absent. The second type does not. The footnote explains that the 'original permissibility' is based on general Quranic verses that allow marriage except where prohibited. Thus, the Hanafi position is that marriage is originally permissible, and the absence of a specific prohibition does not change that.

Key Terms
الحل الأصلي (al-hall al-asli): Original permissibility – the default legal status of an action in the absence of a prohibiting evidence. In marriage, it means that all women are permissible to marry except those explicitly prohibited by Quran or Sunnah.
العلة (al-illah): The effective cause or reasoning behind a legal ruling. The text discusses whether a descriptive attribute functions as an illah that determines the ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The absence of rain automatically permits going out.
B) The original permissibility of going out remains; the condition does not create a prohibition.
C) The condition always negates permissibility when absent.
D) Going out is prohibited regardless of rain.

Key Takeaway
The original permissibility of marriage remains unless a specific prohibition is proven; absence of an attribute does not itself create a prohibition.

The Consensus on Marrying a Free Woman Over a Slave Woman and the Limit of Four Wives
Ruling with proof: two consensus-based marriage rules and refutation of deviant views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on marrying a free woman when one already has a slave wife, and explain the consensus on the maximum number of free wives.

Core Idea
A free man may marry a free woman on top of a slave wife, and cannot exceed four free wives – both rulings are ijma’.

This passage addresses two foundational marriage rulings in the Hanafi school. First, the permissibility of a free man marrying a free woman even if he already has a slave wife. Second, the absolute limit of four free wives. The commentator asserts that both rulings are by consensus (ijma’) of the scholars, and strongly rejects deviant interpretations that allow more than four or unlimited wives.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
Read the matn: 'والحرة على الأمة' and 'وأربع من حرائر'. The first permits marrying a free woman over a slave wife; the second limits free wives to four.
These are stated as categorical permissions and prohibitions.
2
Recognize the evidence: ijma‘ and hadith
The commentator explicitly states 'بالإجماع' for both. He also cites a hadith of ‘A’isha (with a weak chain) that directly says 'يتزوج الحرّة على الأمة'.
The hadith is used as supporting evidence, not primary proof, since the ruling is already founded on consensus.
3
Understand the hadith’s content
The hadith also covers rulings on slave divorce, waiting period, and the prohibition of a slave man marrying a free woman over a slave wife.
The full hadith is: طلاق العبد اثنتان، ولا تحل له حتى تنكح زوجاً غيره، وقرء الأمة حيضتان، ويتزوج الحرّة على الأمة، ولا يتزوج الأمة على الحرة.
4
Distinguish consensus from deviant views
The commentator refutes Shi‘a (who claim nine wives by adding 2+3+4), Khawarij (who claim eighteen by repetition), and al-Shawkani (who permits unlimited wives). He labels these as ignorant of Arabic and consensus.
This demonstrates the importance of proper usul al-fiqh and Arabic understanding.
5
Apply the ruling to practical scenarios
A free man with a slave wife may marry a free woman; a free man with four free wives may not marry a fifth free woman. A slave man cannot marry a free woman over a slave wife.
These rulings are mufta bihi and universally accepted in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[۱) قوله : والحرة على الأمة ؛ هذا وكذا قوله : «أربع» «وحبلى» «وموطوءة» ومن ضمت معطوف على المحرم: أي يجوز نكاح الحرة على الأمة بأن كانت أمة منكوحة فنكح حرة، وهذا بالإجماع. ويشهده حديث عائشة رضي الله عنها : مرفوعاً: «طلاق العبد اثنتان، ولا تحل له حتى تنكح زوجاً غيره، وقرء الأمة حيضتان، ويتزوج الحرّة على الأمة، ولا يتزوج الأمة على الحرة»، أخرجه الدارقطني بسند ضعيف (۳) ، ومثله روى عبد الرزاق عن جابر. [٢]قوله : وأربع من حرائر ؛ هذا حكم مجمع عليه، ولا عبرة لمخالفة بعض الشيعة القائلين بإباحة التسع أخذا من قوله : فَانكِحُوا مَا طَابَ لَكُم مِّنَ النِّسَاءِ مَثْنَى وَثُلَثَ وَرُبَعَ (۳) بجمع اثنين مع ثلاثة وأربعة، وبعض الخوارج القائلين بإباحة ثمانية عشر لجمع هذه الأعداد مع التكرار، فإن هذا المعنى لا يستفاد من الآية، ولا يفهمه إلا الأحمق الذي لا علم له بمجاورة العرب ومعاني الألفاظ. وقد زاد بعض أفاضل اليمن، وهو محمد بن علي الشَّوْكَانِي تبعاً لبعض سفهاء الظاهرية نغمته في الطنبور ، فأباح نكاح النساء من غير حصر، وبينه في بعض تصانيفه بما يستقبحه كل فاضل، وتبعَهُ مَن تبعه من علماء عصرنا (4) ظنَّا أن أقواله كلها نازلةٌ مَنْزِلَةَ الوحي من السماء، وقد رددت عليها في رسالتي تذكرة الراشد برد تبصرة الناقد» إن (۱) ينظر: نصب الراية (٥ : ٢٠٢). (۲) النساء : من الآية. (۳) المقصود به هو صديق حسن خان صاحب «أبجد العلوم»، وغيره.
And the free woman upon the slave woman, and four from free women upon his prohibition. [1] His statement: 'And the free woman upon the slave woman'; this and like his statement: 'four' and 'pregnant' and 'cohabited' and what is included are conjuncts to the prohibited: i.e., it is permissible to marry a free woman over a slave woman if there is a married slave woman, then he married a free woman, and this is by consensus. And it is witnessed by the hadith of 'A'isha, may Allah be pleased with her, raised: 'The divorce of a slave is two, and she is not permissible to him until she marries another husband, and the waiting period of a slave woman is two menses, and the free man marries the free woman over the slave woman, and the slave woman is not married over the free woman.' Reported by al-Daraqutni with a weak chain, and similar is narrated by 'Abd al-Razzaq from Jabir. [2] His statement: 'And four from free women'; this is a consensus ruling, and no attention is paid to the opposition of some Shi'a who claim permissibility of nine, taking from His statement: 'So marry what seems good to you of women, two and three and four' by adding two with three and four, and some Khawarij who claim permissibility of eighteen by adding these numbers with repetition, for this meaning is not derived from the verse, and only a fool who has no knowledge of Arab adjacency and meanings of words understands it. And some eminent men of Yemen, namely Muhammad ibn 'Ali al-Shawkani, following some fools of the Zahiri school, added his tune to the tanbur, so he permitted marrying women without limit, and explained it in some of his writings in a way that every virtuous person finds objectionable, and he was followed by those who followed him among the scholars of our time, thinking that all his sayings descend to the level of revelation from heaven, and I have refuted them in my treatise 'Tadhkirat al-Rashid bi Radd Tabsirat al-Naqid'.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage is a clear ruling statement with supporting evidence from consensus and hadith. The first ruling: a free man may marry a free woman even if he already has a slave woman as a wife. This is permissible by consensus (ijma'). The commentator cites a hadith from 'A'isha, albeit with a weak chain, that a free man can marry a free woman over a slave woman, but a slave man cannot marry a slave woman over a free woman. The second ruling: a man may not marry more than four free women. This is also a consensus ruling. The commentator then refutes deviant views: the Shi'a who claim nine wives by misinterpreting 'two and three and four' as adding them together (2+3+4=9), and the Khawarij who claim eighteen by repeating the numbers. He harshly criticizes these interpretations as ignorant of Arabic usage. He also criticizes al-Shawkani and his followers for allowing an unlimited number of wives, calling their views objectionable. The commentator asserts that these are clear rulings of consensus, and anyone who opposes them is either ignorant or following deviant opinions.

Key Terms
الإجماع (al-ijma'): Consensus of the scholars (mujtahids) of the Ummah on a legal ruling. The text states that both rulings are by consensus, meaning there is no valid disagreement among the recognized schools.
على حرمته (ala hurmatihi): Upon his prohibition – meaning that the attribute (being a free woman) is upon prohibition? Actually the text says 'على حرمته' but the commentary explains it as part of the ruling that the free woman is over the slave woman in terms of status. Possibly a textual corruption.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Up to eighteen, following the Khawarij view.
D) Unlimited, as per al-Shawkani's opinion.

Key Takeaway
A free man may marry a free woman on top of a slave wife, and may not marry more than four free wives – both rulings are established by consensus and supported by hadith.

Marrying a Free Woman Over a Slave Wife
States ruling and consensus with hadith evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on marrying a free woman over a slave wife, cite the consensus and hadith evidence, and explain why it is permissible.

Core Idea
A free man may marry a free woman even if he already has a slave wife – by consensus.

This passage addresses a specific scenario in classical Islamic marriage law: when a man already has a slave woman as a wife, can he marry a free woman? The Hanafi school, relying on consensus and a prophetic hadith, answers yes. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its evidence, and its implications for practical fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is marrying a free woman while already married to a slave woman. The ruling is permissible (mubah).
2
Locate the dalil
The text cites a hadith from Aisha (marfu) and mentions consensus (ijma) as evidence.
The hadith is from al-Daraqutni with a weak chain, but it is supported by a similar narration from Abd al-Razzaq and by consensus.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The explicit phrase 'يتزوج الحرّة على الأمة' directly indicates permissibility of marrying a free woman over a slave wife.
4
Note the consensus
The commentator states that this ruling is by consensus (ijma), meaning all scholars agree, which strengthens the ruling despite the weak hadith chain.
5
Evaluate the authority weight
Although the hadith is ahad and weak in transmission, the consensus makes the ruling reliable and mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( والحرة على الأمة، وأربع من حرائر )

قوله : والحرة على الأمة؛ هذا وكذا قوله : «أربع» «وحبلى» «وموطوءة» ومن ضمت معطوف على المحرم: أي يجوز نكاح الحرة على الأمة بأن كانت أمة منكوحة فنكح حرة، وهذا بالإجماع. ويشهده حديث عائشة رضي الله عنها : مرفوعاً: «طلاق العبد اثنتان، ولا تحل له حتى تنكح زوجاً غيره، وقرء الأمة حيضتان، ويتزوج الحرّة على الأمة، ولا يتزوج الأمة على الحرة»، أخرجه الدارقطني بسند ضعيف (۳) ، ومثله روى عبد الرزاق عن جابر.
(And the free woman over the slave woman, and four free women.) He said: "And the free woman over the slave woman": this, and likewise his statement "four", "pregnant", and "a woman who has been intercourse with" - and whatever is joined with them is a conjunct to the prohibited (women): meaning it is permissible to marry a free woman over a slave woman if there is a slave woman who is a wife, and he marries a free woman, and this is by consensus. And it is attested by the hadith of A'ishah (may Allah be pleased with her), raised (to the Prophet): "The divorce of a slave is two, and she is not lawful for him until she marries another husband, and the waiting period of a slave woman is two menstrual cycles, and a free man may marry a free woman over a slave woman, and he may not marry a slave woman over a free woman." Narrated by al-Daraqutni with a weak chain, and similarly narrated by Abd al-Razzaq on the authority of Jabir.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. The matn states that a free man may marry a free woman even if he already has a slave wife, and he may marry up to four free women. The commentator first explains the phrase "the free woman over the slave woman": it means that if a man has a slave woman as a wife, he is permitted to marry a free woman in addition, and this is established by consensus (ijma'). The evidence cited is a hadith from Aisha (marfu') which explicitly permits this and prohibits the reverse (marrying a slave woman over a free woman). Although the chain is weak, the commentator notes that it is supported by a similar narration from Abd al-Razzaq. The ruling is straightforward: a free man's marriage to a free woman is valid and superior to a slave wife, and he may combine them.

Key Terms
حرة (ḥurrah): A free woman, as opposed to a slave woman (umm walad or concubine).
أمة (amah): A female slave, whether owned or a concubine (umm walad).

Limit of Four Free Wives
Establishes the maximum of four free wives via Quran and Sunnah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maximum number of free wives a free man may marry according to the Hanafi school and cite the Quranic and hadith evidence for this limit.

Core Idea
A free man may marry up to four free women; this limit is established by Quran, Sunnah, and consensus.

This lesson addresses a foundational ruling in marriage (nikah): the maximum number of free wives a free man may simultaneously marry. The Hanafi school, based on the Quran and numerous hadiths, holds that the limit is four. This lesson will examine the evidence and refute misinterpretations that would allow more.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? Marrying free women. What is the ruling? A free man may marry up to four free women. This is explicitly stated in the matn and confirmed by consensus.
The ruling is mubah (permissible) up to four; beyond that is haram.
2
Locate the primary dalil
The Quranic verse 4:3 is the primary evidence. The key phrase is 'فَانكِحُوا مَا طَابَ لَكُم مِّنَ النِّسَاءِ مَثْنَى وَثُلَثَ وَرُبَعَ' (marry what is good for you of women, two, three, and four).
The verse is qat'i al-thubut (definitely from Allah) but zanni al-dalalah (its meaning is open to interpretation).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The majority, including the Hanafis, understand that the words 'مثنى وثلاث ورباع' are not cumulative (i.e., not 2+3+4=9) but rather represent alternative options. A man may choose to marry two, three, or four, but the maximum is four.
This linguistic interpretation is based on Arab usage and is reinforced by the practice of the Prophet.
4
Note the hadith evidence
Multiple authentic hadiths confirm this limit. The most prominent is the story of Ghilan al-Thaqafi, who had ten wives upon embracing Islam and was ordered by the Prophet to keep only four. Similar narrations exist for men with eight or five wives.
These hadiths come from various companions and are reported by multiple authorities, strengthening their reliability.
5
Understand the rejection of deviant views
The commentator rejects interpretations that claim the verse permits nine (by addition) or eighteen (by doubling) or unlimited wives. These views are dismissed as ignorant of Arabic usage and contrary to the consensus of the scholars.
Consensus (ijma') itself is a binding proof in Hanafi usul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وأربع من حرائر؛ هذا حكم مجمع عليه، ولا عبرة لمخالفة بعض الشيعة القائلين بإباحة التسع أخذا من قوله : فَانكِحُوا مَا طَابَ لَكُم مِّنَ النِّسَاءِ مَثْنَى وَثُلَثَ وَرُبَعَ (۳) بجمع اثنين مع ثلاثة وأربعة، وبعض الخوارج القائلين بإباحة ثمانية عشر لجمع هذه الأعداد مع التكرار، فإن هذا المعنى لا يستفاد من الآية، ولا يفهمه إلا الأحمق الذي لا علم له بمجاورة العرب ومعاني الألفاظ. وقد زاد بعض أفاضل اليمن، وهو محمد بن علي الشَّوْكَانِي تبعاً لبعض سفهاء الظاهرية نغمته في الطنبور ، فأباح نكاح النساء من غير حصر، وبينه في بعض تصانيفه بما يستقبحه كل فاضل، وتبعَهُ مَن تبعه من علماء عصرنا (4) ظنَّا أن أقواله كلها نازلةٌ مَنْزِلَةَ الوحي من السماء، وقد رددت عليها في رسالتي تذكرة الراشد برد تبصرة الناقد» إن شئت الإطلاع فلترجع إليها.
وقد ثبت ما ذهب إليه الجمهور بالقرآن وبالأحاديث، فأخرج الشافعي وابن أبي شَيْبة وأحمد والترمذي وابن ماجة وغيرهم عن ابن عمر ان غيلان الثقفي : «أسلم وتحته عشر نسوة، فقال له النبي : اختر منهن أربعاً (٢). وفي رواية: «أمسك أربعاً وفارق سائرهن (۳). وأخرج ابن أبي شيبة وغيره عن قيس بن الحارث الأسدي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال : «أسلمت وكان تحتي ثمـان نسوة فأتيت رسول الله له فأخبرته فقال : اختر منهنَّ أربعاً وخـلّ سائرهن»(4). وأخرج الشافعي عن نوفل بن معاوية ، قال : «أسلمت وتحتي خمس نسوة، فقال النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم: أمسك أربعاً وفارق الأخرى (م). وأخرج ابن جرير وابن المنذر وغيرهما عن ابن عباس له في تفسير قوله لا فَانكِحُوا مَا طَابَ لَكُم مِّنَ النِّسَاءِ مَثْنَى وَثُلَثَ وَرُبَعَ (۱) قصر الرجال على أربع نسوة وأخرج سعيد بن منصور وعبد بن حميد وغيرهما نحوه عن سعيد بن جبير وفي الباب أخبار وآثار أخر، وضعف أسانيد بعضها لا يضر لإنجباره بكثرة الطرق.
His statement: "And four from free women" – this is a ruling agreed upon by consensus, and no consideration is given to the opposition of some Shi'a who permit nine, taking from His statement: "marry what is good for you of women, two, three, and four" by combining two with three and four, and some Kharijites who permit eighteen by combining these numbers with duplication. For this meaning is not derived from the verse, and no one understands it except a fool who has no knowledge of the usage of the Arabs and the meanings of expressions. And one of the virtuous of Yemen, Muhammad ibn Ali al-Shawkani, following some foolish literalists (Zahiriyya), added his tune to the tambour, permitting marriage to women without restriction, and explained it in some of his writings in a way that every virtuous person finds objectionable, and those who followed him from the scholars of our time thought his statements all descend as revelation from heaven. I have refuted this in my treatise 'Tadhkirat al-Rashid bi-Radd Tabsirat al-Naqid' – if you wish to see it, refer to it. And what the majority went to has been established by the Quran and by hadiths. Al-Shafi'i, Ibn Abi Shayba, Ahmad, al-Tirmidhi, Ibn Majah and others narrated from Ibn 'Umar regarding Ghilan al-Thaqafi: "He embraced Islam and had ten wives, so the Prophet said to him: 'Choose four of them.'" In another narration: "Keep four and separate from the rest." Ibn Abi Shayba and others narrated from Qays ibn al-Harith al-Asadi who said: "I embraced Islam and I had eight wives, so I came to the Messenger of Allah and informed him, and he said: 'Choose four of them and leave the rest.'" Al-Shafi'i narrated from Nawfal ibn Mu'awiya who said: "I embraced Islam and I had five wives, so the Prophet said: 'Keep four and divorce the other.'" Ibn Jarir, Ibn al-Mundhir, and others narrated from Ibn 'Abbas in the interpretation of His statement: "marry what is good for you of women, two, three, and four" that it restricts men to four women. Sa'id ibn Mansur, Abd ibn Humayd, and others narrated similarly from Sa'id ibn Jubayr. And in this chapter there are other narrations and traditions, and the weakness of the chains of some does not harm due to being compensated by the multitude of paths.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with extensive proof. The matn restricts a free man to four free wives, and the commentator confirms this is a matter of consensus (ijma'). He refutes deviant interpretations from some Shi'a (who claim nine wives by misinterpreting the Quranic phrase 'two, three, four' as 2+3+4=9) and some Kharijites (who claim eighteen by doubling these numbers). He strongly criticizes Muhammad al-Shawkani for his literalist view allowing unlimited wives. The commentator then provides the correct evidence: the Quranic verse 4:3 is interpreted by the majority, including Ibn Abbas, to mean a maximum of four. He also cites multiple hadiths where the Prophet commanded men with more than four wives upon embracing Islam to reduce to four. The multiplicity of narrations compensates for any weakness in individual chains, establishing the limit firmly.

Key Terms
حرائر (hara'ir): Free women (plural of ẖurrah); women who are not slaves.
مجمع عليه (mujma' 'alayh): A ruling agreed upon by consensus of the scholars.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the maximum number of free women a free man may marry at one time?
A) Nine
B) Eighteen
C) Four
D) Unlimited

Key Takeaway
The maximum number of free wives a free man may have is four, established by Quran, Sunnah, and consensus.

Marrying Up to Four Slave Women
States permission to marry up to four slave women

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the maximum number of slave women a free man may marry and identify the evidence that the limit of four applies to all women.

Core Idea
A free man may marry up to four slave women, just as he may up to four free women—the limit is universal.

We have seen that the Quran and Sunnah limit a man to four wives. The Hanafi school extends this ruling to include slave women, concluding that a man cannot marry more than four women regardless of their status. This lesson confirms that the same numerical limit applies, and you will understand the proof from the generality of the Quranic command and the Prophet's instructions to those who embraced Islam with multiple wives.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what act is being ruled upon (marrying slave women) and the hukm level (mubah, permissible).
The ruling is not obligatory but permitted, with a maximum of four.
2
Understand the linguistic point
The word 'إماء' is the plural of 'أمة' meaning female slave. The commentator explains this to clarify the subject of the ruling.
This linguistic clarification is important because the term is less common.
3
Recognize the evidence (dalil)
The passage cites the generality of the Quranic verse ('marry what is good for you of women, two, three, and four') and hadiths where the Prophet limited men with many wives to four upon embracing Islam.
The evidence supports that the limit applies to all women, free or slave.
4
Note unanimity among schools
The Hanafi position that a free man may marry up to four slave women is not disputed by other schools (muttafaq alayh).
While details differ, the numerical limit is agreed upon.
5
Apply the ruling in context
A free man with existing free wives cannot simply add slave wives beyond the total of four. The total of all wives (free and slave) must not exceed four.
This application follows from the general principle.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement followed by supporting evidence (though the evidence seems to be repeated from the previous section). The matn states 'وإماء' (and slave women), meaning that a free man may marry up to four slave women, just like free women. The commentator explains that the word 'إماء' is the plural of 'أمة' (female slave), and the ruling is derived from the general application of the Quranic verse and hadith that limit the number of wives to four, without distinction between free and slave. The hadiths cited (about men with many wives upon embracing Islam being reduced to four) apply to women generally, whether free or slave. However, note that the hadiths are repeated from the discussion on free women, indicating that the same limit applies.

Ruling on the Number of Wives for a Slave
Clarifying that a slave may marry only two wives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the maximum number of wives for a slave, explain the dalil from Quran and ijma', and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
A slave may marry two wives, half of a free man’s four, due to the effect of slavery on legal capacity.

In this passage, the author explains the matn 'wa al-abd nisfuha', establishing that a slave can only marry two wives. This ruling is derived from Quranic context, the principle that slavery halves rights, and the consensus of the Companions. Understanding it requires analyzing the Quranic verse, addressing the objection of general applicability, and examining the specific evidence from the Salaf.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Clearly articulate the Hanafi ruling: a male slave may marry up to two wives, whether free or slave. This is the mufta bihi position.
The ruling is mubah (permissible) but capped at two.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Identify that Imam al-Shafi'i holds that a slave may marry only one wife, and only under conditions of fearing sin and lacking means to marry a free woman.
The Shafi'i position is more restrictive.
3
Follow the Hanafi proof
Examine the dalil: Quran 4:3 is addressed to free men due to the context of concubinage (milk al-yamin). Additionally, the Companions reached ijma' that a slave cannot exceed two wives.
The Hanafi proof combines textual and consensual evidence.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to the opponent
The Shafi'i argue the verse is general. The Hanafi response: the verse is restricted by context, and the ijma' of the Companions overrides the general apparent meaning.
The Hanafi response relies on the principle that context can specify a general text.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
State the practical consequence: according to Hanafis, a slave can have two valid marriages; according to Shafi'is, only one marriage is valid (if at all).
This difference affects the validity of a slave's second marriage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وإماء؛ بكسر الهمزة جمع أمة؛ أي نكاح أربع إماء؛ لإطلاق قوله تعالى: ﴿فَانكِحُوا مَا طَابَ لَكُم مِّنَ النِّسَاءِ مَثْنَى وَثُلَثَ وَرُبَعَ﴾، فإن لفظ النساء شامل للحرائر والإماء فيقتصر الحكم على أربع من الجنسين، نعم له أن يتخذ من الجواري ما شاء من العدد. فإن قلت: كما أن النساء مطلق كذلك الخطاب: فَانكِحُوا أيضاً مطلق غير مخصص بالأحرار، فيلزم أن يباح هذا العدد المذكور للعبيد أيضاً كما يباح للأحرار، وهو المروي عن مالك رحمه الله. قلت: الخطاب في فَانكِحُوا في الآية السابقة ليس بمطلق، بل للأحرار فقط بدليل قوله تعالى: ﴿فَإِنْ خِفْتُمْ أَلَّا تَعْدِلُوا فَوَحِدَةً أَوْ مَا مَلَكَتْ أَيْمَانُكُمْ ذَلِكَ أَدْنَى أَلَّا تَعولُوا﴾، فلا دلالة للآية على جواز نكاح أربع للعبيد، نعم هو حجة على الشافعي في قوله: لا يتزوج إلا أمة واحدة أي عند خوف العنت وعدم طول الحرة. [١]قوله: نصفها؛ أي يجوز له نكاح الاثنتين حرتين كانتا، أو أمتين فقط؛ لأن الحل الثابت بالنكاح مشترك بين الزوجين حتى أن للمرأة المطالبة بالاستمتاع، وقد نَصَّفَ الرِّق المرأة ما لها من الحل حتى إذا كانت تحت الرجل حرة وأمة يكون للحرة ليلتان وللأمة ليلة، فلما نَصَّفَ رقها ما لها وجب أن يُنصف رفه ما له، فللحر تزوج أربع وللعبد تزوج اثنتين. كذا في «فتح القدير» وقد أخرج ابن أبي شَيْبَة والبيهقي عن الحكم، قال: «أجمع الصحابة رضي الله عنهم على أن لا ينكح العبد أكثر من اثنتين»، وعن عمر رضي الله عنه: «ينكح العبد امرأتين»، أخرجه الشافعي، وكذا عن علي رضي الله عنه وعبد الرحمن بن عوف، وقال الشافعي: لا نعرف لهم من الصحابة رضي الله عنهم مخالفاً.
His saying: 'and slave-women' – with kasra on the hamza, plural of slave-woman; meaning the marriage of four slave-women, due to the absolute nature of His statement: 'then marry such women as seem good to you, two or three or four.' Indeed, the word 'women' includes free women and slave-women, so the ruling is limited to four from both categories; yes, he may take as many concubines as he wishes. If you say: just as 'women' is absolute, so too the address 'marry' is absolute and not restricted to free men, so it would follow that this mentioned number is also permissible for slaves just as it is permissible for free men, and this is narrated from Malik (may Allah have mercy on him). I say: the address in 'marry' in the previous verse is not absolute, but rather for free men only, due to His statement: 'then if you fear that you will not be just, then one [wife] or what your right hands possess. That is more likely that you will not do injustice.' So the verse does not indicate the permissibility of marrying four for slaves; rather it is an argument against al-Shafi'i in his statement that he (the slave) may marry only one slave-woman, i.e., when fearing fornication and lacking the means to marry a free woman. [1] His saying: 'and for the slave is half of that' – meaning he may marry two, whether they are free women or only slave-women, because the right established by marriage is shared between the spouses such that the woman can demand enjoyment, and slavery halves the right for the woman, so if she is under a man with a free and a slave-wife, the free woman has two nights and the slave-woman one night. Thus, when slavery halved what she has, it is necessary that it halve what he has. So the free man marries four and the slave marries two. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. And Ibn Abi Shayba and al-Bayhaqi narrated from al-Hakam: 'The Companions (may Allah be pleased with them) agreed that a slave should not marry more than two.' And from Umar (may Allah be pleased with him): 'The slave may marry two women,' as al-Shafi'i narrated, and similarly from Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) and Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf. And al-Shafi'i said: 'We do not know of any Companion who opposed them.'
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning the maximum number of wives a male slave may contract in marriage. The author begins by explaining the matn 'wa imaa' (and slave-women), which refers to the permissibility of marrying slave-women. However, the key ruling is that a slave—like a free man—is limited to a total of four wives from both free and slave categories, based on Quran 4:3. But the author then addresses an objection: if the verse is general, why wouldn't slaves also be allowed four wives? The response is that the verse's address is specifically to free men, as indicated by the context of fearing injustice and the alternative of concubinage (milk al-yamin). This argument is used against Imam al-Shafi'i, who held that a slave may marry only one wife (and only to avoid sin). The author then explains the matn 'wa al-abd nisfuha' (and for the slave is half of that), i.e., a slave can marry only two wives. The reasoning is based on the effect of slavery: just as a slave-wife's right to her husband's time is halved compared to a free wife, so too the slave husband's capacity is halved. This is supported by the consensus (ijma') of the Companions, as transmitted by al-Hakam, and by specific rulings of Umar, Ali, and Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf. Importantly, al-Shafi'i himself acknowledges that no Companion dissented. Thus, the Hanafi position is firmly established.

Key Terms
إماء (ima'): Plural of amah (female slave). Refers to slave-women whom a free man may marry, up to four total including free women.
نصف (nisf): Half. Here it means the slave's marital capacity is half that of a free man (two wives instead of four).
رق (riqq): Slavery. In this context, it legally halves the right to marriage (for both male and female slaves).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many wives may a male slave marry according to the Hanafi school?
A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) Four

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rules that a male slave may marry two wives, based on the effect of slavery halving legal capacity, Quranic contextual interpretation, and the consensus of the Companions.

A Slave's Marriage Limit: Two Wives
Explaining the slave's marriage limit of two wives

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a slave's marriage limit and explain its proofs from the Qur'an, analogy, and Companions' consensus.

Core Idea
A slave may marry two wives, half a free man's limit, due to halving of rights and Companions' consensus.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains why a male slave is limited to two wives, half of a free man's four. The author navigates a potential objection from the apparent generality of Qur'an 4:3, provides an analogy based on the halving of rights, and seals the argument with a reported Consensus of the Companions. By understanding this lesson, you will see how the Hanafi school harmonises textual proof, analogical reasoning, and early community practice.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Begin by identifying the exact ruling: a male slave may marry up to two wives, whether both are free or both are slave-girls.
This is the 'mufta bihi' position.
2
Analyse the Qur'anic proof
Locate the citation of Qur'an 4:3. Note that while the word 'women' is general, the address 'marry' is contextually restricted to free men, as evidenced by the later phrase 'or what your right hands possess'.
This refutes the objection that the verse applies to slaves.
3
Trace the analogical reasoning
Examine the analogy: just as a slave woman's right to her husband's time is halved (free wife gets two nights, slave wife one), so a slave man's capacity to marry is halved. Identify the original case (asl), the new case (far'), and the effective cause (illa).
The illa is the effect of slavery on rights.
4
Confirm with Companions' consensus
Review the narrations from Umar, Ali, and Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf, and the explicit statement that the Companions unanimously agreed that a slave may not marry more than two.
Ijma' is a binding source of law in Hanafi usul.
5
Assess the cross-madhhab dispute
Compare with the Shafi'i position: a slave may marry only one slave-girl, and only under duress. Identify the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) and why the Hanafi view is relied upon.
The Hanafi response uses contextual analysis and consensus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللعبد نصفها ) : فَانكِحُوا مَا طَابَ لَكُم مِّنَ النِّسَاءِ مَثْنَى وَثُلَثَ وَرُبَعَ ) (١) ، فإن لفظ النساء شامل للحرائر والإماء فيقتصر الحكم على أربع من الجنسين، نعم له أن يتخذ من الجواري ما شاء من العدد.

فإن قلت : كما أن النِّساء مطلق كذلك الخطاب : فَانكِحُوا أيضاً مطلق غيرُ مخصص بالأحرار، فيلزم أن يباح هذا العدد المذكور للعبيد أيضاً كما يباح للأحرار، وهو المروي عن مالك لله.

قلت : الخطاب في فَانكِحُوا في الآية السابقة ليس بمطلق، بل للأحرار فقط بدليل قوله ان بعد قوله: ﴿ وَرُبَعَ فَإِنْ خِفْتُمْ أَلَّا نَعْدِلُوا فَوَحِدَةٌ أَوْ مَا مَلَكَتْ أَيْمَنُكُمْ ذَلِكَ أَدْنَى أَلَّا ، فلا دلالة للآية على جواز نكاح أربع للعبيد، نعم هو حجة على الشافعي

تعولوا
(۲)
ه في قوله : لا يتزوج إلا أمة واحدة أي عند خوف العنت وعدم طول الحرة. [١]قوله: نصفها ؛ أي يجوز له نكاح الاثنتين حرتين كانتا ، أو أمتين فقط ؛ لأن الحل الثابت بالنكاح مشترك بين الزوجين حتى أن للمرأة المطالبة بالاستمتاع، وقد نَصَّفَ الرِّق المرأة ما لها من الحل حتى إذا كانت تحت الرجل حرة وأمة يكون للحرة ليلتان وللأمة ليلة، فلما نَصَّفَ رقها ما لها وجب أن يُنصف رفه ما له، فللحر تزوج أربع وللعبد تزوج اثنتين. كذا في «فتح القدير» وقد أخرج ابن أبي شَيْبَة والبيهقي عن الحكم ، قال : «أجمع الصحابة لأن على أن لا ينكح العبد أكثر من اثنتين ، وعن عمر الله : ينكح العبد امرأتين ) ، أخرجه الشافعي، وكذا عن علي () وعبد الرحمن بن عوف ، وقال الشافعي : لا نعرف لهم من الصحابة له مخالفاً.
And for the slave half of it. (The verse:) 'Marry what is lawful to you of women, two, three, and four.' So the word 'women' includes free women and slave-girls, so the ruling is restricted to four from both genders. Indeed, he may take as many slave-girls as he wishes. If you say: Just as 'women' is absolute, so the address 'marry' is also absolute, not restricted to free men, so this number would be permissible for slaves as well as for free men, and that is reported from Malik. I say: The address in 'marry' in the previous verse is not absolute, but specifically for free men, evidenced by His saying after 'and four': 'And if you fear you will not be just, then one, or what your right hands possess.' So the verse does not indicate the permissibility of marrying four for slaves; rather it is an argument against al-Shafi'i in his saying: 'A slave may marry only one slave-girl, when fearing unlawful sex and lacking the means for a free woman.' [1] His saying: 'half of it' means: it is permissible for him to marry two, whether both are free or both are slave-girls, because the right established by marriage is shared between the spouses such that the woman may demand enjoyment. And slavery halves what a woman has of right, so if a man has a free wife and a slave wife, the free wife gets two nights and the slave wife one night. Since her slavery halves what she has, it becomes necessary to halve what he has. Thus a free man may marry four, and a slave may marry two. So it is in Fath al-Qadir. Ibn Abi Shayba and al-Bayhaqi narrated from al-Hakam: 'The Companions unanimously agreed that a slave may not marry more than two.' And from Umar: 'A slave may marry two women.' Al-Shafi'i narrated it, and similarly from Ali and Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf. Al-Shafi'i said: 'We know no Companion who opposed them.'
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling that a slave may marry only two wives, half of a free man's limit of four. The text begins by citing the Qur'anic verse (4:3) which permits marrying up to four women, using the general word 'women' which includes both free and slave. The author initially restricts this to free women but then addresses an objection: if 'women' is general, and the command 'marry' is also general, then slaves should also be allowed four, as reported from Imam Malik. The Hanafi response clarifies that the address in the verse is specifically for free men, as evidenced by the subsequent part of the verse where after mentioning four, it says 'if you fear injustice, then one, or what your right hands possess' – this option of slave-girls indicates that the initial ruling of up to four applies only to those who can also marry slave-girls, which is free men. Thus the verse does not prove permissibility for slaves; rather, it supports the Hanafi position against al-Shafi'i, who restricts a slave to only one slave-girl. The commentator then explains the meaning of 'half of it' by citing a reasoning from symmetry: the rights in marriage are mutual, and just as a slave woman's right to her husband's time is halved compared to a free wife (free wife gets two nights, slave wife one), so a slave man's capacity to marry is halved. The text further supports the ruling with a report of Ijma' (consensus) of the Companions that a slave may not marry more than two, and narrations from Umar, Ali, and Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf, with al-Shafi'i acknowledging no opponent.

Key Terms
نصفها (nisfuha): Meaning that a slave's marriage limit is half that of a free man, i.e., two wives.
إجماع الصحابة (ijma' al-sahaba): Consensus of the Companions, cited here to prove that a slave may marry only two.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view stated in this passage, how many wives may a male slave marry?
A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) Four

Key Takeaway
A slave's marriage limit is two wives, established by contextual Qur'anic interpretation, analogy from halving of rights, and Companions' consensus.

Marrying a Pregnant Woman from Zina and a Mawtu'ah
Rulings on marrying a pregnant adulteress and a mawtu'ah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on marrying a pregnant adulteress and a mawtu'ah, distinguish between conditions for marriage and intercourse, and identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

Core Idea
Marriage to a pregnant adulteress is valid; intercourse is haram until delivery.

This lesson unpacks a key ruling from the Hanafi school: a woman pregnant from fornication may be married, but intercourse is prohibited until she delivers. The passage also covers a slave-girl who has been used sexually by her master (mawtu'ah) – her marriage is valid without obligatory istibra'. We will examine the proofs and the disagreement of Abu Yusuf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases
The passage first addresses a pregnant adulteress (hubla min zina) and then a slave-girl who has been intercourseed by her master (mawtu'atu sayyidiha). Note the ruling for each.
Both rulings involve marriage validity and post-marriage intercourse.
2
Extract the ruling for hubla min zina
The woman may be married, but intercourse is forbidden until delivery. Distinguish between the permissibility of the contract and the prohibition of intercourse.
This differs from a woman pregnant from lawful marriage (in iddah) where marriage is prohibited.
3
Analyze the dalil
The author cites Quranic verses to prohibit marriage during iddah (from lawful pregnancy) and to prohibit intercourse to avoid lineage confusion. Understand how these proofs apply only to lawful pregnancy.
The prohibition of intercourse does not entail prohibition of marriage, as shown by analogy with muhrima, ha'id, nufasa'.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf prohibited marriage entirely. The majority (Abu Hanifa, Muhammad) permit marriage but forbid intercourse until delivery. The mufta bihi is the majority view.
The disagreement hinges on whether the prohibition of intercourse implies prohibition of marriage.
5
Apply to mawtu'ah
Marrying a slave-girl used by her master is permissible. Istibra' is not obligatory (wajib) but is recommended (mustahabb) as caution. Muhammad recommended waiting one menstrual cycle.
Istibra' differs from iddah; it is only a recommended precaution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحُبْلَى من زنا ولا توطأ حتى تضع حملها، ومَوْطُوءة سيِّدِها ، أو زان وحُبلى من زنا ولا توطأ حتى تضع حملها ) ، ومَوْطُوءة سيِّدِها ، أو زان)، أي يجوزُ نكاح أمةٍ وطئها سيدها ، ولا يجب على الزوج الاستبراء
[١] قوله : وحبلى مـن زنـا ؛ وقيد به إشارة إلى أنه لو كانت حاملة من غير زنا لا يحل نكاحها؛ لأنها في العدة ؛ لقوله : وَأُوْلَتُ الْأَعْمَالِ أَجَلُهُنَّ أَن يَضَعْنَ حملَهُنَّ ) ، والنكاح في العدّة ممنوع لقوله : وَلَا تَنْزِمُوا عُقْدَةَ النِّكَاحِ حَتَّى يبْلُغَ الْكِتَبُ أَجَلَهُ (۳). والحاصل أن الحاملة بالزنا يجوزُ نكاحها ، ولا يجوز وطؤها حتى تضع حملها، وخالف أبو يوسف له في جواز النكاح ولا حجة له، فإن الوطء إنما منع ; لئلا يلزمُ سقي حرث غيره بمائه المؤدِّي إلى فساد النسب ، وليس من ضرورة حرمة الوطء حرمة النكاح ، كما في نكاح المحرمة والحائض والنفساء.
[٢] قوله : ولا توطأ ؛ أي لا يحل وطء الحبلى من زنا المنكوحة إلى وضع الحمل، وكذا لا تباح دواعيه، وهل تستحق النفقة فيه قولان والأوجه لا ; لأن النفقة وإن وجبت بالعقد الصحيح لكن إذا لم يكن مانع من الدخول من جهتها، وهذا يضاف إلى فعلها الزنا بخلاف الحائض، فإن عذرها سماوي، وهذا كله إذا كان الناكح غير الزاني ، فإن نكح الزاني بحبلى من زنا منه جاز النكاح اتفاقاً ، وحل بالوطء أيضاً. كذا في «فتح القدير»
[٣] قوله : ولا يجب ; أشار به إلى أن المنفي هو الوجوب دون الاستحباب كما قال محمد الله: لا أحب أن يطأها قبل أن يستبرئها، وقوله : أقرب إلى الاحتياط. كذا في «البناية»
And a pregnant woman from zina: she is not to be sexually intercourseed until she delivers her child. And a slave-girl who has been intercourseed by her master, or a fornicator, [and a pregnant from zina is not intercourseed until she delivers]. That is, it is permissible to marry a slave-girl whom her master has intercourseed, and the husband is not required to perform istibra'. [1] His saying: 'And a pregnant from zina': He restricted it to indicate that if she were pregnant from other than zina, it would not be permissible to marry her because she is in her waiting period (iddah). Allah says: 'And those who are pregnant, their term is when they deliver their burden.' And marriage during iddah is prohibited because Allah says: 'And do not conclude the marriage contract until the prescribed term reaches its end.' The conclusion is that a pregnant woman from zina may be married, but she may not be intercourseed until she delivers. Abu Yusuf disagreed with him on the permissibility of marriage, but he has no proof. Intercourse is prohibited only to avoid watering another's crop with his semen, leading to confusion of lineage. And the prohibition of intercourse does not necessitate the prohibition of marriage, as in the case of marrying a woman in ihram, a menstruating woman, or a postpartum woman. [2] His saying: 'And she is not to be intercourseed': meaning that it is not permissible to intercourse a married pregnant woman from zina until she delivers, and likewise her intimacies are not permitted. As to whether she is entitled to maintenance, there are two opinions; the preferred view is no, because maintenance, though obligatory due to a valid contract, is [only due] when there is no impediment to intercourse from her side, and this impediment stems from her act of zina, unlike a menstruating woman whose excuse is heavenly. All this is if the husband is not the fornicator. If the fornicator marries a woman pregnant from his own zina, marriage is permissible by agreement, and intercourse becomes permissible as well. So it is in Fath al-Qadir. [3] His saying: 'And he is not required': he indicated that the negation is of obligation, not recommendation, as Muhammad said: 'I do not like that he intercourse her before she performs istibra', and his saying: 'It is closer to caution.' So it is in al-Binaya.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage addresses two related rulings: first, marrying a pregnant woman from zina; second, marrying a slave-girl who has been intercourseed by her master (mawtu'atus sayyidiha). The text first states that a pregnant adulteress may not be intercourseed until she delivers. It then clarifies that if the pregnancy is from a lawful marriage (i.e., she is in iddah), marriage is prohibited. The key point: a pregnant woman from zina may be married (unlike if she were pregnant from a lawful husband, as she would be in iddah). However, intercourse is forbidden until delivery to avoid confusion of lineage. The commentator notes that Abu Yusuf differed, prohibiting marriage altogether, but his view is weak. The commentator compares to marrying a muhrima, ha'id, or nufasa' where intercourse is forbidden but marriage is valid. Next, the text discusses a slave-girl who has been intercourseed by her master: it is permissible to marry her, and the new husband is not required to perform istibra' (waiting for one menstrual cycle to ensure the womb is clear). The commentator notes that the negation of obligation leaves room for recommendation, quoting Muhammad ibn al-Hasan who recommended istibra' as caution. The passage also addresses the case where the fornicator himself marries the woman he impregnated: marriage is valid by consensus, and intercourse is permissible.

Key Terms
حبلى من زنا (hubla min zina): A woman pregnant as a result of fornication, not from a lawful marriage.
موطوءة سيدها (mawtu'atu sayyidiha): A slave-girl with whom her master has had sexual intercourse.
استبراء (istibra'): A waiting period to ascertain that the womb is empty (usually one menstrual cycle).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Permissible immediately
B) Prohibited until she delivers
C) Prohibited forever
D) Permissible after istibra'

Key Takeaway
A pregnant woman from zina can be married, but intercourse is haram until delivery; a mawtu'ah slave-girl can be married without obligatory istibra'.

Nikah — Marriage

Marrying a Pregnant Woman from Zina
Ruling with proof on marriage and intercourse with a woman pregnant from zina

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on marrying a woman pregnant from zina, explain the distinction between marriage and intercourse, and identify the intra-Hanafi dispute with Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
A marriage with a woman pregnant from zina is valid, but intercourse is forbidden until delivery to protect lineage.

This lesson clarifies a nuanced ruling: a woman impregnated through zina is not in iddah and can be married, but the couple must wait until after delivery for intercourse. The proof comes from Quranic verses on iddah and the prohibition of sex during iddah. Abu Yusuf disagreed on marriage permissibility, but the Hanafi school follows Abu Hanifa's view, supported by analogy with other prohibitions that do not bar the marriage contract itself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what act is under discussion (marrying a woman pregnant from zina) and the hukm levels: marriage is permissible (mubah), intercourse is haram until delivery.
The act is the marriage contract itself, not the intercourse.
2
Locate the dalil
Examine the Quranic evidence: Surah At-Talaq 65:4 (pregnant women's iddah ends at birth) and Surah Al-Baqarah 2:235 (prohibition of marriage during iddah). The passage uses these to argue that a pregnancy from zina does not create iddah, so marriage is allowed.
The verses are cited directly in the text.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Understand how the dalil proves the ruling: the iddah verses apply only to lawful pregnancies; zina does not establish lineage, so the woman is not in iddah. Hence, the prohibition on marriage during iddah does not apply.
The analogy is that forbidding intercourse does not forbid marriage, as in cases of ihram or menstruation.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Identify the intra-Hanafi disagreement: Abu Yusuf held that marriage itself is impermissible, while Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani allow it. Understand his reasoning and the Hanafi response.
Abu Yusuf's view is rejected; the mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The commentary dismisses Abu Yusuf's proof, stating that prohibition of intercourse does not entail prohibition of marriage, citing analogies (ihram, menstruation). The relied-upon position is that marriage is valid and intercourse must wait.
This is a classic example of distinguishing between the contract and its consummation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحُبْلَى من زنا ولا توطأ حتى تضع حملها، ومَوْطُوءة سيِّدِها ، أو زان) ، أي يجوزُ نكاح أمةٍ وطئها سيدها ، ولا يجب على الزوج الاستبراء. [١] قوله : وحبلى مـن زنـا ؛ وقيد به إشارة إلى أنه لو كانت حاملة من غير زنا لا يحل نكاحها؛ لأنها في العدة ؛ لقوله : وَأُوْلَتُ الْأَعْمَالِ أَجَلُهُنَّ أَن يَضَعْنَ حملَهُنَّ ) ، والنكاح في العدّة ممنوع لقوله : وَلَا تَنْزِمُوا عُقْدَةَ النِّكَاحِ حَتَّى يبْلُغَ الْكِتَبُ أَجَلَهُ (۳). والحاصل أن الحاملة بالزنا يجوزُ نكاحها ، ولا يجوز وطؤها حتى تضع حملها، وخالف أبو يوسف له في جواز النكاح ولا حجة له، فإن الوطء إنما منع ؛ لئلا يلزمُ سقي حرث غيره بمائه المؤدِّي إلى فساد النسب ، وليس من ضرورة حرمة الوطء حرمة النكاح ، كما في نكاح المحرمة والحائض والنفساء. [٢] قوله : ولا توطأ ؛ أي لا يحل وطء الحبلى من زنا المنكوحة إلى وضع الحمل، وكذا لا تباح دواعيه، وهل تستحق النفقة فيه قولان والأوجه لا ؛ لأن النفقة وإن وجبت بالعقد الصحيح لكن إذا لم يكن مانع من الدخول من جهتها، وهذا يضاف إلى فعلها الزنا بخلاف الحائض، فإن عذرها سماوي، وهذا كله إذا كان الناكح غير الزاني ، فإن نكح الزاني بحبلى من زنا منه جاز النكاح اتفاقاً ، وحل بالوطء أيضاً. كذا في «فتح القدير». [٣] قوله : ولا يجب ؛ أشار به إلى أن المنفي هو الوجوب دون الاستحباب كما قال محمد الله: لا أحب أن يطأها قبل أن يستبرئها، وقوله : أقرب إلى الاحتياط. كذا في «البناية»
And a pregnant woman from fornication, and she is not to be touched until she delivers her child, and a slave woman touched by her master, or a fornicator..., meaning: it is permissible to marry a slave woman whom her master has touched, and istibra' is not obligatory upon the husband. [1] His statement: 'And a pregnant woman from fornication'; he restricted it to indicate that if she were pregnant from other than fornication, her marriage is not permissible because she is in 'iddah, due to His statement: 'And those who are pregnant, their term is until they deliver their burden' (65:4), and marriage during 'iddah is prohibited due to His statement: 'And do not resolve the marriage contract until the prescribed term reaches its end' (2:235). The conclusion is that a pregnant woman from fornication: her marriage is permissible, but intercourse is not permissible until she delivers her child. Abu Yusuf disagreed with him regarding the permissibility of marriage, but he has no proof. For intercourse is only prevented so as not to water the cultivation of another with one's water, leading to corruption of lineage, and the prohibition of intercourse does not necessitate the prohibition of marriage, as in the case of marriage of a woman in ihram, a menstruating woman, or a woman in post-childbirth bleeding. [2] His statement: 'And she is not to be touched'; meaning: it is not permissible to have intercourse with a married pregnant woman from fornication until after delivery, and likewise her pleasures are not permissible. There are two opinions regarding whether she deserves maintenance, and the preponderant view is no, because maintenance, although established by a valid contract, is conditional upon there being no impediment to intercourse from her side, and this is attributable to her act of fornication, unlike a menstruating woman whose excuse is heavenly. All of this applies when the husband is not the fornicator. If the fornicator marries a woman pregnant from his own fornication, marriage is permissible by consensus, and intercourse is also permissible. This is as in Fath al-Qadir. [3] His statement: 'And it is not obligatory' indicates that what is negated is obligation, not recommendation, as Muhammad said: 'I do not like that he touch her before he observes istibra', and his statement is closer to precaution. This is as in al-Binayah.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage addresses the permissibility of marrying a woman impregnated through fornication (zina) and the rulings regarding intercourse and istibra'. The matn states that a pregnant woman from zina may be married but not touched until she delivers, and a slave woman who has been touched by her master may also be married without the husband needing to observe istibra'. The commentary clarifies that if the pregnancy is from a legitimate source (e.g., marriage), the woman is in 'iddah and cannot be married; proof from the Quran establishes that pregnant women's 'iddah ends at birth, and marriage during 'iddah is forbidden. The ruling for zina is different because the pregnancy is not attributed to a lawful husband. Abu Yusuf disagreed on the permissibility of marriage itself, but the author dismisses his view, noting that prohibition of intercourse does not entail prohibition of marriage. Regarding maintenance, there is disagreement; the preferred view is that she does not deserve it because the impediment to intercourse from her side (the pregnancy resulting from her own sinful act) prevents the obligation, unlike a natural impediment like menstruation. However, if the fornicator marries the same woman he impregnated, both marriage and intercourse are permissible by consensus. Finally, istibra' (waiting for one menstrual cycle to ensure the womb is empty) is not obligatory for the husband, but it is recommended; Muhammad al-Shaybani considers it closer to precaution. The lesson combines textual proof (Quran), rationale (avoiding lineage confusion), and inter-madhhab dispute (Abu Yusuf).

Key Terms
حبلى (hubla): A pregnant woman
استبراء (istibra'): Waiting for one menstrual cycle to ensure the womb is empty of pregnancy
عدة (iddah): The waiting period after divorce or death of husband before a woman can remarry

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) No, it is not permissible because she is in iddah.
B) Yes, it is permissible but intercourse is prohibited until she delivers.
C) Yes, it is permissible and intercourse is also permissible immediately.
D) It is makruh tahrimi.

Key Takeaway
A woman pregnant from zina is not in iddah, so marriage is valid, but intercourse must wait until delivery to protect lineage.

Nikah — Marriage

Marriage Contract Combining a Prohibited Woman: Validity for the Lawful Wife
Ruling on marrying two women in one contract where one is prohibited

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a prohibited woman is joined in the same marriage contract and explain the legal basis for the permissible woman's marriage being valid.

Core Idea
If a prohibited woman is included in one marriage contract, the permissible woman's marriage remains valid.

In Nikah, contracts can involve multiple parties. What happens if one of the women in a single contract is permanently prohibited for the man? Does the entire contract collapse, or can it be split? This lesson addresses that precise scenario, clarifying that the prohibition attaches only to the individual, not to the entire transaction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and category
The act is a marriage contract (nikah) involving two women simultaneously in one agreement. Categorise it as a single contract with multiple subject women.
The contract is one ‘aqd, not two separate contracts.
2
Identify the prohibited woman
One woman is muharramah – permanently prohibited for marriage (e.g., by blood, fosterage, or affinity). Determine the basis of her prohibition.
Permanent prohibition means she can never become lawful for this man.
3
State the ruling for each woman
The marriage of the prohibited woman is invalid (batil). The marriage of the permissible woman is valid (sahih) and effective.
The contract is split: the prohibition only taints the prohibited party, not the other.
4
Recognise the consequence
The permissible woman is entitled to her dower (mahr) upon consummation. The prohibited woman gets nothing and must be separated immediately.
This differs from sales, where joining a corrupt element voids the entire sale.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومَن ضُمَّت إلى مُحَرَّمة ) : أي إذا تزوج امرأتين بعقد واحد وإحداهما محرَّمةٌ عليه صح نكاحُ الأُخرى
And one who is joined to a prohibited woman: that is, if a man marries two women with one contract and one of them is prohibited to him, the marriage of the other is valid.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This is a brief ruling: if a man marries two women in a single marriage contract, and one of them is prohibited to him (e.g., his foster sister or a woman already married to him), the marriage is not entirely void. Instead, the marriage of the permissible woman (the one who is not prohibited) is valid and effective. This differs from sale, where joining a prohibited item invalidates the entire sale because sale is invalidated by corrupt conditions. Marriage is more flexible: the prohibited woman's mention is considered void (laghw), and the entire dower goes to the permissible woman. If the husband consummates the marriage, she is entitled to mahr al-mithl (fair dower). This ruling highlights that marriage contracts are not automatically invalidated by partial prohibitions; only the affected part is severed.

Key Terms
محرَّمة (muharramah): A woman who is permanently prohibited for marriage (e.g., mahram by blood, fosterage, or affinity)
ضم (damma): To join or include in the same contract

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) The contract is valid for both, but the foster sister must be divorced immediately.
D) The marriage of the non-mahram woman is invalid until the foster relationship ends.

Key Takeaway
In a single marriage contract joining a prohibited and a permissible woman, the permissible woman's marriage remains valid; the prohibition does not invalidate the entire contract.

Prohibition of a Master Marrying His Own Slave Woman
Ruling on master marrying his own female slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling that a master cannot marry his own female slave and explain the rational basis for the prohibition.

Core Idea
A master cannot marry his own slave woman because marriage rights conflict with ownership rights.

This lesson examines a fundamental ruling in Hanafi nikah: the prohibition of a master marrying his own female slave. The text from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains that even partial ownership invalidates the marriage contract. The reasoning is that marriage creates mutual rights (such as maintenance and submission) that are incompatible with the master's absolute ownership. Understanding this conflict is key to grasping the boundaries of marital contracts in Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling
Begin by recognizing the clear ruling: a master cannot marry his slave woman, even if he owns only a part of her.
This includes co-ownership scenarios.
2
Understand the purpose of marriage
Marriage is legislated to produce shared rights (thamarāt) between spouses, including financial and physical entitlements.
These rights are mutual and define the marital relationship.
3
Identify the conflicting rights
List the exclusive rights of the wife (maintenance, housing, division of nights, prevention of coitus interruptus) and of the husband (submission for intimacy), as well as shared rights (physical enjoyment).
These rights presuppose the wife is a free person, not owned.
4
Apply the conflict
Since the woman is owned by the master, she cannot simultaneously hold marital rights against him — ownership and marriage are incompatible.
Thus the contract is batil (void) from inception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا نكاح أمته ) [٤] قوله : لا نكاح أمته ؛ أي لا يجوز نكاح السيد أمته ولو ملك بعضها ؛ لأن النكاح ما شرع إلا مثمراً ثمرات مشتركة في الملك بين المتناكحين : منها : ما تختص هي بملكه : كالنفقة والسكنى والقسم والمنع من العزل. ومنها : ما يختص هو بملكه : كوجوب التمكين. ومنها : ما يكون مشتركاً كالاستمتاع مجامعة ومباشرة. ومن المعلوم أن المملوكة تنافي المالكية، فلا يصح جعل مملوكته منكوحة مالكيه.
'No marriage of his slave woman.' [4] His statement: 'No marriage of his slave woman'; meaning: it is not permissible for a master to marry his slave woman, even if he owns a part of her, because marriage was only legislated to produce shared rights in ownership between the spouses: among them what is exclusively hers to own: such as maintenance, housing, division (of nights), and prevention of coitus interruptus. Among them what is exclusively his to own: such as the obligation of submission. Among them what is shared: such as enjoyment through intercourse and direct contact. And it is known that the female slave contradicts ownership, so it is not valid to make his possessed women a wife of her owner.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This ruling states that a master cannot marry his own female slave, even if he owns only a part of her. The reason is that marriage is designed to create mutual rights between spouses, which are incompatible with the rights of ownership. The commentary lists the rights that each spouse owns: the wife has exclusive rights to maintenance, housing, allotted nights, and the right to prevent coitus interruptus; the husband has exclusive rights to the wife's submission for intimacy; and they share the physical enjoyment. Since a slave woman is owned by the master, she cannot simultaneously be his wife because ownership overrides the rights that a wife would have. For example, if she were his wife, she would have the right to demand maintenance and housing, but as a slave, she is already entitled to maintenance from her master, but not as a marital right. More fundamentally, the marriage contract's purpose of creating joint property rights conflicts with the master's absolute ownership. Therefore, the contract is not valid. This rule applies even if the master owns only a partial share of the slave woman, as ownership in part still creates a conflict of interest.

Key Terms
أمة (amah): A female slave owned by a master
تمكين (tamkin): The wife's obligation to submit to her husband for intimate relations

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is it not permissible for a master to marry his own slave woman?
A) Because marriage requires witnesses, which cannot be provided.
B) Because the rights of marriage conflict with the rights of ownership.
C) Because the slave woman must consent separately.
D) Because the master cannot afford the mahr.

Key Takeaway
A master cannot marry his own female slave because the rights created by marriage are fundamentally incompatible with the rights of ownership.

Ruling on Combining a Lawful Woman with a Forbidden in One Contract
Hanafi ruling on marriage validity when lawful woman combined with forbidden

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a lawful woman and a forbidden woman are married in one contract and explain the distinction from sales contracts.

Core Idea
A valid marriage remains valid even if bundled with a void one; the forbidden part is disregarded.

This passage from a Hanafi commentary addresses a specific marriage scenario: a man marries two women in a single contract, one of whom is permanently forbidden to him (e.g., his sister). The ruling clarifies that the marriage of the lawful woman is fully valid, while the forbidden one is void. Understanding this ruling requires appreciating how Hanafi fiqh treats conditions in marriage differently from sales.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is marrying two women in one contract, one lawful and one forbidden. The ruling: the lawful woman's marriage is sahih (valid), and the forbidden woman's marriage is batil (void).
The ruling only concerns the validity of each woman's marriage, not the entire contract.
2
Understand the reasoning (ta'lil)
The lawful marriage is valid because there is no legal impediment regarding her. The forbidden woman's part is considered a null condition that does not affect the other.
Contrast with sales, where an invalid condition voids the entire transaction.
3
Distinguish from sales
In a sale, if a lawful item is combined with an unlawful item, the entire sale becomes void because sales are invalidated by invalid conditions. Marriage is different: it is not invalidated by such conditions.
This is a key principle: marriage contracts are more robust than commercial contracts.
4
Determine the dower consequences
The entire specified dower (mahr musamma) goes to the lawful woman because the mention of the forbidden woman is void. If consummation occurs, she receives mahr al-mithl if the specified dower is invalid.
The forbidden woman gets nothing, as her marriage is void from the start.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومَن ضُمت إلى مُحَرَّمة : أي إذا تزوج امرأتين بعقد واحد وإحداهما محرَّمةٌ عليه صح نكاحُ الأُخرى. قوله : صح نكاح الأخرى ; لعدم مانع في حقها بخلاف ما إذا ضُمَّ في البيع مع المبيع حيث يفسد البيع ; لأن البيع يفسد بالشروط الفاسدة، ولا كذلك النكاح، ثمّ المهر المسمى في النكاح يكون كله للمحلّلة ; لكون ذكر المحرمة لغواً، فإن دخل بها بعد النكاح فلها مهر المثل.
And whoever combines [a lawful woman] with a forbidden one: meaning if he marries two women with one contract and one of them is forbidden to him, the marriage of the other is valid. His statement: 'the marriage of the other is valid' because there is no impediment regarding her, contrary to when [a lawful item] is combined with an unlawful item in a sale, for the sale becomes invalid because sales are invalidated by invalid conditions; but marriage is not like that. Then the dower specified in the marriage goes entirely to the lawful woman because the mention of the forbidden one is void; if he consummates with her after the marriage, she receives the dower of the like.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the Hanafi school on a specific scenario in marriage contracts. The matn (base text) states that if a man marries two women in a single contract, one of whom is forbidden to him (e.g., his mahram or an already married woman), the marriage of the lawful woman is valid while the forbidden one is void. The commentator (sharh) expands: the reason the valid one's marriage is sound is that there is no legal impediment concerning her. He contrasts this with sales: if a lawful item is bundled with an unlawful item in one sale, the entire sale is void because sales are invalidated by invalid conditions. However, marriage is not invalidated by the inclusion of a void condition; only the unlawful part is void, and the lawful part stands. Therefore, the entire specified dower (mahr musamma) goes to the lawful wife, as the mention of the forbidden woman is treated as null and void. If the husband consummates the marriage with the lawful woman after the contract, she is entitled to mahr al-mithl (the dower of the like) if the specified dower was not validly assigned. This is based on the principle that marriage contracts are not as easily invalidated by invalid conditions as sales are.

Key Terms
ضُمت (ḍummat): In this context, 'combined' meaning joining two women in one marriage contract.
مُحَرَّمة (muḥarramah): A woman who is permanently forbidden in marriage due to kinship, suckling, or marriage ties.
مهر المثل (mahr al-mithl): The appropriate dower for a woman of her status if no valid dower was specified.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man marries two women in one contract, one lawful and one forbidden (his sister), what happens to the marriage of the lawful woman?
A) Both marriages are void.
B) The lawful woman's marriage is valid, the forbidden one's is void.
C) The lawful woman's marriage is valid but she gets no dower.
D) The entire contract is suspended until the forbidden woman is divorced.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a marriage contract is not invalidated by including a forbidden woman; the lawful part stands, and the entire specified dower goes to the lawful wife.
