Prohibition of Marrying One's Own Female Slave
Ruling on the invalidity of a master marrying his own female slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a master marrying his own female slave and explain the legal reasoning based on the incompatibility of ownership and marriage rights.

Core Idea
Ownership and marriage are mutually exclusive: a master cannot marry his slave.

The Hanafi school firmly rules that a master cannot marry his own female slave, even if he owns only a share of her. This is not merely a matter of consent or procedure; the very structure of marriage—built on mutual rights and obligations—is fundamentally incompatible with the unilateral power of ownership. Let's explore the precise reasoning behind this prohibition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
The passage begins by stating the ruling: 'There is no marriage of his female slave'—meaning it is not permissible. Explicitly, the marriage is invalid (batil) and prohibited (haram).
2
Recognize the Scope
The ruling applies even if the master owns only part of the slave (ولو ملك بعضها). The incompatibility arises from any degree of ownership.
Thus, partial ownership does not mitigate the prohibition.
3
Analyze the Rationale (Ta'lil)
Marriage was legislated to produce shared rights (ثمرات مشتركة) between spouses. The passage categorizes these rights into three types: those the husband possesses (maintenance, housing, division of nights, prohibition of coitus interruptus), those the wife possesses (obligation of submission), and those shared (physical intimacy).
The list illustrates that marriage requires a balanced partnership.
4
Establish the Contradiction
Being a female slave (مملوكية) contradicts being a master/owner (مالكية). A slave, by definition, lacks the legal capacity to own or owe rights independently. Thus, she cannot fulfill the wife's obligations (e.g., submission is not a true right if she is already under ownership). Similarly, the master cannot simultaneously owe duties to his property.
The term تنافي المالكية captures this mutual exclusion.
5
Conclude the Invalidity
Because of this irreconcilable conflict, it is not valid (لا يصح) to make one's female slave the wife of her master. The contract is void ab initio.
The ruling is absolute; no exceptional circumstances can validate it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا نكاح أمته ; أي لا يجوز نكاح السيد أمته ولو ملك بعضها ; لأن النكاح ما شرع إلا مثمراً ثمرات مشتركة في الملك بين المتناكحين : منها : ما تختص هي بملكه : كالنفقة والسكنى والقسم والمنع من العزل. ومنها : ما يختص هو بملكه : كوجوب التمكين. ومنها : ما يكون مشتركاً كالاستمتاع مجامعة ومباشرة. ومن المعلوم أن المملوكة تنافي المالكية، فلا يصح جعل مملوكته منكوحة مالكيه.
There is no marriage of his female slave: meaning it is not permissible for a master to marry his female slave, even if he owns only a share of her. Because marriage was only legislated to produce shared rights between the spouses: among them, what is specifically owned by the husband (like maintenance, housing, division of nights, and prohibition of coitus interruptus); and among them, what is specifically owned by the wife (like obligation of submission); and among them, what is shared (like enjoyment through intercourse and physical contact). And it is known that being a slave girl contradicts being a master (owner). So it is not valid to make his female slave the wife of her owner.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is a rule from the Hanafi school stating the absolute prohibition of a master marrying his own female slave, even if he only owns a partial share of her. The reasoning (ta'lil) is that marriage was legislated to produce shared rights and mutual obligations between husband and wife, which require a certain degree of equality and independence. Rights such as maintenance, housing, division of nights, and prohibition of coitus interruptus are the husband's responsibility. Rights such as the wife's submission to her husband are her duty. Shared rights include sexual intimacy. However, in the case of a master and his slave girl, the relationship of ownership (malikiyyah) is fundamentally opposite to the relationship of marriage. Ownership gives the master power over the slave, while marriage requires a partnership of rights. Therefore, marriage between a master and his own slave is invalid. This ruling applies even if the master owns only a part of the slave, as the incompatibility remains.

Key Terms
أمَة (amah): A female slave owned by a master.
مملوكية (mamlukiyyah): The state of being owned as a slave.
تنافي المالكية (tanāfi al-mālikiyyah): The mutual exclusion between being owned and being an owner in the context of marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the slave's consent cannot be properly obtained.
B) Because the rights of marriage conflict with the rights of ownership.
C) Because the slave may be pregnant from another man.
D) Because the master's religion differs from the slave's.

Key Takeaway
A master cannot marry his own female slave because the rights of ownership and the rights of marriage are fundamentally incompatible, rendering the contract void (batil).

No Istibra Required When Marrying a Fornicated Woman
Ruling with proof by legal reason (taʿlīl)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on marrying a woman who committed zina without istibra and explain the legal reason (ʿillah) for this ruling.

Core Idea
Zina semen has no legal effect, so no istibra is required upon marriage.

This passage from ʿUmdat al-Riʿāyah clarifies a specific but important subsidiary ruling in nikah: if a man wishes to marry a woman who has previously engaged in zina (fornication) with another man, he may do so without requiring her to observe istibra (waiting for one menstrual period). The reasoning is rooted in a foundational Hanafi principle: the semen from zina is not legally recognized for establishing lineage. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its condition, and its contrast with istibra in other contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
The act is marrying a woman who has committed zina with another man. The ruling is that the marriage is valid without the obligation of istibra.
The ruling applies only if she is not pregnant from that zina.
2
Identify the Condition
The condition is that the woman must not be pregnant from the zina. The Arabic phrase 'الغير الحامل' (the non-pregnant) makes this explicit.
3
Identify the ʿIllah (Legal Reason)
The reason given is 'لعدم اعتبار ماء الزنا شرعاً' – because the semen of zina is not legally considered. In Hanafi law, lineage is only established through valid marriage or acknowledgment; zina produces no legal lineage, so there is no need to wait to determine potential pregnancy.
This ʿillah is a legal principle, not a textual proof from Quran or Sunnah.
4
Contrast with Istibra in Halal Relations
Compare with the case of a female slave being sold: the buyer must observe istibra because the previous owner’s halal intercourse could establish lineage. The difference is exactly this legal recognition of the semen.
This comparison is not in the immediate passage but is standard in Hanafi fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله وكذا ; أي يصح نكاحُ الزانية الغير الحامل من غير وجوب الاستبراء على الزوج لعدم اعتبار ماء الزنا شرعاً.
His statement 'and similarly' means: the marriage of a fornicatress who is not pregnant is valid without the obligation of istibra (waiting for a menstrual period) upon the husband, because the semen of zina is not legally considered (for establishing lineage).
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage explains a subsidiary ruling in the Hanafi school: If a man wishes to marry a woman who has previously committed zina (fornication) with another man, he may do so without requiring her to undergo istibra' (a waiting period of one menstrual cycle to ensure she is not pregnant). The reason is that, according to Islamic law, the child conceived through zina is not attributed to the fornicator; the semen of zina has no legal effect regarding lineage. Therefore, there is no need to ascertain whether she is pregnant from that illegal act. This is in contrast to the case of a female slave who is sold: the buyer must observe istibra' because the previous owner's legal intercourse (halal) could have caused pregnancy, and that child would be attributed to the owner. The commentator clarifies that this ruling applies only if the woman is not already pregnant from the zina; if she is pregnant, the marriage is not valid until she delivers, due to the possibility of mixing lineages.

Key Terms
استبراء (istibrā'): A waiting period of one menstrual cycle to ensure the womb is empty of pregnancy.
ماء الزنا (mā' al-zinā): The semen from forbidden intercourse, which has no legal effect for lineage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because zina is a minor sin that does not affect marriage.
B) Because the semen from zina is not legally considered for establishing paternity.
C) Because she is not considered divorced.
D) Because the previous man has no right over her.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits immediate marriage to a woman who committed zina, without istibra, because zina semen carries no legal lineage.

Prohibition of a Slave Marrying His Mistress
Ruling with proof via talil

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for a male slave marrying his female owner and explain the legal rationale (talil) using the principle of incompatibility of ownership.

Core Idea
A male slave cannot marry his owner because the status of being owned negates the capacity to be an owner in marriage.

The text presents a clear ruling from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah: a male slave is absolutely forbidden from marrying his female mistress. This is not merely discouraged but void (batil). The underlying reason is that the slave's condition of being owned (mamlukiyyah) is fundamentally incompatible with the rights and obligations that come with being a spouse, especially the wife's duties of submission and shared intimacy. We will examine the ruling and its proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is a male slave marrying his female owner. The ruling is haram and the marriage contract is batil (void).
2
Understand the dalil
The proof is given by the phrase 'because being owned conflicts with being an owner.' This is a talil (legal rationale) showing the incompatibility of statuses.
The conflict is not just physical but legal: the slave cannot simultaneously be owned and exercise ownership rights in marriage.
3
Note the consensus
The ruling is agreed upon by ijma (consensus). There is no valid scholarly difference on this point.
This means the rule is absolute and not subject to ikhtilaf.
4
Apply the ruling to partial ownership
Even if the slave is partially owned by the mistress (e.g., a mudabbar or a part-slave), the rule still applies because the conflict exists.
The ruling is absolute; any degree of ownership invalidates the marriage.

Source Text

And his mistress: meaning it is not permissible for a male slave to marry his female owner, because being owned conflicts with being an owner.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This is a clear ruling from the Hanafi school that a male slave cannot marry his female mistress (owner). The reason is the same as in the previous ruling: the state of being a slave (mamlukiyyah) is incompatible with being an owner (malikiyyah) in the context of marriage. Since the slave is owned by the mistress, the rights and obligations of marriage cannot be properly established because ownership gives the mistress authority over the slave, while marriage requires a partnership of rights. The commentator notes that this is agreed upon by consensus (ijma') that such a marriage is batil (void). The specific rights that would be compromised include the wife's obligation to submit, the husband's custody, and shared intimacy—all of which are negated by the master-slave relationship.

Key Terms

مالكية (malikiyyah): Ownership; the right of possession.

Prohibition of Marrying a Magian Woman
Ruling that a Muslim man cannot marry a Magian woman

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on marrying a Magian woman and explain why the opposing evidence is rejected.

Core Idea
Marriage to a Magian woman is haram because she is not Ahl al-Kitab; the contrary narration is weak.

In this lesson, we examine the prohibition of a Muslim man marrying a Magian (Zoroastrian) woman. The Hanafi school holds it is haram based on the consensus of the majority of scholars (ijma al-jumhur). A minority opinion, attributed to Dawud al-Zahiri, permits it, citing a weak narration from Ali (ra). We will analyze the evidence and see why the Hanafi position is the mufta bihi.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position and its basis
The ruling is haram. The basis is that Magians are not Ahl al-Kitab; the Quran only permits marriage to Muslim and Kitabi women (Surah al-Ma'idah 5:5). The majority of scholars (jumhur) are agreed on this prohibition.
This ijma is a strong evidence.
2
Identify the opposing view and its evidence
Dawud al-Zahiri and his followers permit marriage to Magians, based on a narration from Ali (ra) that Magians once had a scripture and thus should be considered People of the Book.
3
Analyze the strength of the opposing evidence
The narration is weak (da'if) in its chain of transmission, as established by Ibn Hajar al-Asqalani in Talkhis al-Habir.
A weak narration cannot establish a ruling that contradicts clear evidence.
4
Apply the Hanafi principle of evidence weighting
In Hanafi usul, a weak hadith is not sufficient to override clear Quranic text and ijma. The consensus of the jumhur is qat'i, while the Zahiri evidence is zanni and weak.
The principle: 'al-thabitu bi al-yaqin la yazulu bi al-shakk'.
5
Conclude with the mufta bihi ruling
The prohibition remains the operative ruling (mufta bihi). A Muslim man may not marry a Magian woman.
This is the established Hanafi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمجوسية ; هي التي تعبد النارَ ; لأن الجائز إنما هو نكاح أهل الكتاب لا غيره من الكفّار، وعليه أجمع جمهور المسلمين. وشد داود الظاهري ومن وافقه في تجويزه أخذاً بما أخرجه الشافعي عن علي له : «أنّ المجوس كانوا مـن أهـل كتاب وأن ملكهم سَكِرَ فَوَقَعَ على ابنته أو أخته فاطلع عليه بعض أهل مملكته ، فلما أصبح جاؤوا ليقيموا عليه الحدّ فامتنع منهم، فدعى أهل مملكته فقال : تعلمون ديننا خير من دین آدم اللي قد كان آدم الة ينكح بنينه من بناته ، فأنا على دين آدم ، وما نرغب بكم عن دينه فبايعوه على ذلك، وقاتلوا من خالفهم، فأصبحوا وقد أُسري على كتابهم فرفع من بين أظهرهم ، وذهـب العـلـم الـذي في صدورهم، ولا حجة لهم فيه، فإن سنده ضعيف، كما بسطه ابن حجر في «تلخيص الحبير»
And the Magian woman: she is one who worships fire; because what is permissible is only marriage with the People of the Book, not with other disbelievers, and upon this the majority of Muslims are agreed. Dawud al-Zahiri and those who agreed with him dissented, permitting it, based on what al-Shafi'i narrated from Ali (may Allah be pleased with him): 'The Magians were once People of the Book, and their king became drunk and raped his daughter or sister. Some of his subjects witnessed it. In the morning they came to establish the punishment but he prevented them. He called his people and said: Do you know that our religion is better than the religion of Adam? Adam used to marry his sons to his daughters, so I am following the religion of Adam. So they pledged allegiance to him on that and fought those who opposed them. Then their scripture was taken up and removed from among them, and the knowledge in their hearts disappeared.' There is no proof in this, for its chain is weak, as Ibn Hajar explained in Talkhis al-Habir.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage discusses the prohibition of a Muslim man marrying a Magian (Zoroastrian/fire-worshipping) woman. The matn states the ruling: it is not permissible. The commentator explains that the Magians worship fire and are not People of the Book; only marriage with Jewish and Christian women is allowable by Quranic permission (Surah al-Ma'idah 5:5). The majority of Muslim scholars (jumhur) are agreed on this prohibition. However, Dawud al-Zahiri and a few others allowed it, citing a narration from Ali (ra) that the Magians originally had a scripture but it was lost due to their king's corruption. The commentary refutes this evidence by stating that the chain of transmission is weak (da'if), as established by Ibn Hajar al-Asqalani. Thus, the Hanafi position is that marriage to Magians is not allowed. This ruling is based on the consensus of the majority (ijma' al-jumhur) and the absence of a valid proof for permissibility.

Key Terms
مجوسية (majūsiyyah): A female follower of the Magian religion (Zoroastrianism), fire-worshipper.
أهل الكتاب (ahl al-kitāb): People of the Book, primarily Jews and Christians, whose marriages are permissible for Muslim men.

Prohibition of a Slave Marrying His Mistress
Ruling with rationale on slave-mistress marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a slave marrying his mistress and explain the legal reasoning based on the mutual rights of marriage and the contradiction with ownership.

Core Idea
Ownership and marriage are incompatible — mutual rights of marriage cannot exist where one spouse owns the other.

This passage addresses the fundamental Hanafi principle that marriage requires reciprocal rights and obligations between spouses. When one party owns the other, such reciprocity is impossible, rendering the marriage contract invalid by consensus.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
Read the passage to determine what action is being ruled upon. The act is a slave marrying his mistress (or a master marrying his slave woman). The ruling is that it is forbidden (haram) and the contract is invalid (batil).
The prohibition covers both directions: master marrying his slave woman and slave marrying his mistress.
2
Identify the basis of the ruling
The passage states that there is consensus (ijma) on the invalidity. Note that this makes the ruling agreed upon across schools, not just Hanafi.
Consensus gives the ruling a high level of certainty.
3
Analyze the rationale
The passage provides a legal reasoning (talil): marriage is legislated to produce mutual fruits—specific rights and obligations for both spouses. Ownership (milk) is a relationship of domination that contradicts the equality needed for these mutual rights. Since the purpose of marriage cannot be achieved, the contract is not legitimate.
The key is that ownership prevents the 'partnership' (shirkah) aspect of marriage.
4
Conclude the legal effect
The conclusion is that such a marriage contract is batil (void) from the outset, and not merely prohibited but inherently invalid.
This means no legal effects follow; the couple must separate immediately.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسيدته (1) أي حرم عليه نكاح أمته وحرم على العبد نكاح سيدته للإجماع على بطلانه؛ ولأن النكاح لم يشرع إلا مثمراً ثمرات مشتركة بين المتناكحين يوجب له عليها التمكين من نفسها وقرارها في بيته وخدمته داخل البيت ويوجب لها عليه المهر والنفقة والسكنى والكسوة والقسم، والمملوكية تنافي المالكية فيمتنع وقوع الثمرة على الشركة فلا يشرع لما عرف أن كل تصرف لا يترتب عليه مقصوده لا يكون مشروعاً; ولأن المقصود من النكاح التواد والإحسان; ومقصود الرق ما سبق منه من الكفر فلا يجتمعان للتضاد.
And his mistress (1) i.e. it is forbidden for a man to marry his slave woman and forbidden for a slave to marry his mistress due to consensus on its invalidity; and because marriage was only legislated to produce mutual fruits between the spouses, which obligates for him her permission over herself and her residence in his house and her service inside the house, and obligates for her the dowry, maintenance, housing, clothing, and division of time, and slave ownership contradicts ownership (mastery) so the occurrence of the fruit on partnership is impossible, so it is not legislated because it is known that every transaction that does not achieve its purpose is not legitimate; and because the purpose of marriage is love and kindness; and the purpose of slavery is the aforementioned humiliation and subjugation due to disbelief, so they cannot combine due to contradiction.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage states the ruling that a slave cannot marry his mistress, and likewise a master cannot marry his female slave. The commentator explains that there is consensus (ijma) on the invalidity of such a marriage. The reasoning is pragmatic: marriage is legislated to create mutual rights and obligations, such as the husband's right to enjoy his wife and her service, and the wife's right to dowry and maintenance. However, when one party owns the other, these mutual rights cannot be actualized because ownership (milk) is a legal relationship of domination, while marriage requires equality and reciprocity. Since the objectives of marriage cannot be achieved, the marriage is invalid.

Key Terms
المملوكية (al-mamlukiyyah): The state of being a slave, belonging to a master.
المالكية (al-malikiyyah): The state of being a master or owner.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because she is a free woman and he is a slave
B) Because marriage requires mutual rights that cannot exist with ownership
C) Because the slave cannot afford the dowry
D) Because the mistress's father would not consent

Key Takeaway
A marriage contract between an owner and his slave (or between a master and his slave) is invalid (batil) because ownership prevents the realization of the mutual rights that are essential to marriage.

Prohibition of Marrying Magian Women
Inter-madhhab dispute on marrying Magian women

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on marrying Magian women and analyse why the Zahiri position is rejected on methodological grounds.

Core Idea
Magians are polytheists, not People of the Book; marriage to them is haram by consensus.

Marriage to non-Muslim women is generally restricted to the People of the Book (Jews and Christians). But what about Zoroastrians, who have a historical claim to a scripture? This passage tackles a sharp inter-madhhab dispute: the Hanafi school forbids it, while the Zahiri school permits it based on a contested narration. Let's analyse the evidence and methodology behind the Hanafi position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi Position
State the ruling: Marriage to Magian women is haram. Magians are polytheists, not Ahl al-Kitab.
Based on consensus (ijma) of the majority of Muslims.
2
Identify the Opposing School and Their Proof
Dawud al-Zahiri permits marriage based on a narration from Ali (ra) suggesting Magians were once People of the Book.
The narration is cited by al-Shafi'i.
3
Analyse the Hanafi Response to the Opposing Dalil
The narration is weak (da'if) in chain. Even if authentic, it refers to a past state; Magians now are polytheists.
Ibn Hajar in Talkhis al-Habir details the weakness.
4
Note the Supporting Hanafi Dalil
A mursal marfu' hadith explicitly prohibits marrying Magian women: 'Do not marry their women nor eat their slaughtered animals.'
Narrated by Ibn Abi Shaybah and Abd al-Razzaq.
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf
Practical consequence: Hanafi school forbids such marriages; Zahiri school permits them. Muslim should follow the Hanafi mufta bihi.
The Hanafi position is stronger due to consensus and clearer proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمجوسية (2) هي التي تعبد النار؛ لأن الجائز إنما هو نكاح أهل الكتاب لا غيره من الكفار، وعليه أجمع جمهور المسلمين.
وداود الظاهري ومن وافقه في تجويزه أخذاً بما أخرجه الشافعي عن علي له : «أن المجوس كانوا من أهل كتاب وأن ملكهم سَكِرَ فَوَقَعَ على ابنته أو أخته فاطلع عليه بعض أهل مملكته، فلما أصبح جاؤوا ليقيموا عليه الحدّ فامتنع منهم، فدعى أهل مملكته فقال : تعلمون ديننا خير من دين آدم اللي قد كان آدم ينكح بنينه من بناته، فأنا على دين آدم، وما نرغب بكم عن دينه فبايعوه على ذلك، وقاتلوا من خالفهم، فأصبحوا وقد أُسري على كتابهم فرفع من بين أظهرهم، وذهب العلم الذي في صدورهم، ولا حجة لهم فيه، فإن سنده ضعيف، كما بسطه ابن حجر في «تلخيص الحبير»
وعلى تقدير صحته نقول : لا نعني بالمجوس إلا عبدة النار، وكونهم كان معهم كتاب لا يفيد شيئاً، فإنه لا شك أنهم الآن داخلون في المشركين، وأيضاً بالرفع والنسيان قد أخرجوا من زمرة أهل الكتاب.
ويشهد له ما أخرجه ابن أبي شيبة وعبد الرزاق عن الحسن بن محمد بن علي مرسلاً مرفوعاً: «إن النبي كتب إلى مجوس نجران من أسلم قبل منه، ومن لم يُسْلِم ضربت عليه الجزية غير ناكحي نسائهم ولا آكلي ذبائحهم».
And the Magian woman (2) is she who worships fire; because only marriage to People of the Book is permissible, not other non-Muslims, and upon that is the consensus of the majority of Muslims. And Dawud al-Zahiri and those who agreed with him in permitting it took as evidence what al-Shafi'i narrated from 'Ali: that the Magians were from the People of the Book, and that their king became drunk and had intercourse with his daughter or his sister, and some people of his kingdom saw him. When morning came, they came to establish the hadd punishment against him, but he refused. He called his people and said: 'Do you know that our religion is better than the religion of Adam? Indeed Adam used to marry his sons to his daughters. So I am on the religion of Adam, and I do not wish to turn you away from his religion.' So they pledged allegiance to him on that, and fought those who opposed them. Then their book was taken away and raised from among them, and the knowledge that was in their hearts disappeared. There is no proof for them in this, for its chain is weak, as Ibn Hajar explained in Talkhis al-Habir. And assuming its authenticity, we say: By 'Magians' we mean only fire-worshippers, and the fact that they once had a book does not benefit, for there is no doubt that they are now included among the polytheists. Moreover, by the removal and abrogation they have been taken out of the category of People of the Book. This is supported by what Ibn Abi Shaybah and Abd al-Razzaq narrated from al-Hasan ibn Muhammad ibn Ali as a mursal marfu' hadith: 'The Prophet (SAW) wrote to the Magians of Najran: Whoever accepts Islam is accepted from him, and whoever does not accept Islam is to have jizyah imposed upon him, but do not marry their women nor eat their slaughtered animals.'
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage addresses the prohibition of marrying Magian (Zoroastrian) women. The Hanafi position, based on consensus (ijma) of the majority, is that marriage is only permissible to women from the People of the Book (Jews and Christians). Magians are not considered People of the Book. A minority view, attributed to Dawud al-Zahiri, holds that Magians are from the People of the Book based on a narration from Ali. The passage examines this narration and finds its chain weak. Moreover, even if the narration were authentic, it does not establish that contemporary Magians are People of the Book, as their original book was abrogated and they are now polytheists. A supporting hadith from the Prophet explicitly prohibits marrying Magian women and eating their slaughtered animals.

Key Terms
المجوس (al-Majus): Fire-worshippers; Zoroastrians.
أهل الكتاب (Ahl al-Kitab): People of the Book; Jews and Christians who have divine scriptures.

Key Takeaway

The Prohibition of Marrying Polytheist Women
Ruling against marrying polytheist women with Quranic proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on marrying polytheist women and explain the Quranic evidence that prohibits it.

Core Idea
Marriage to any polytheist woman is haram until she believes, based on the clear prohibition in Surah al-Baqarah.

In this passage, the commentator explains the scope of the prohibition against marrying polytheist women. While the term 'idolatress' is used, the ruling extends to all forms of polytheism, including star worship and any disbelief outside the People of the Book. The primary evidence is the direct command of Allah in the Quran.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act being ruled upon
The act is marrying a woman who is a polytheist (mushrik), specifically one who worships idols, stars, or any object besides Allah.
The text uses 'idolatress' but clarifies the ruling applies to every non-Muslim.
2
State the ruling (hukm)
The ruling is haram (prohibited) for a Muslim man to marry such a woman.
The prohibition is absolute; conversion to Islam removes the prohibition.
3
Locate the primary evidence (dalil)
The evidence is Quran 2:221: 'And do not marry polytheist women until they believe.' The verse is cited in the source text.
This is a clear, definitive command (nahy) indicating prohibition.
4
Explain the wajh al-dalalah (how the proof shows the ruling)
The imperative 'لا تنكحوا' (do not marry) is a prohibition, which in Hanafi usul indicates haram unless an exception is proven. The exception 'until they believe' confirms the ruling remains haram until faith is acquired.
The ruling is general; it covers all polytheist women, not just idol worshippers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوثنية (1) نسبة إلى وثن: وهو بفتحتين ماله جنة: أي صورة من خشب أو حجر، والمراد بها عابدة الصنم، وكذا الحكم في عباد النجوم وغيرها من الأشياء وكل كافر، والأصل فيه قوله تعالى: {وَلَا تَنكِحُوا الْمُشْرِكَتِ حَتَّى يُؤْمِنَّ} [البقرة: 221] الآية.

And similarly the ruling applies to worshippers of stars and other things, and every non-Muslim. The basis for it is the saying of Allah Most High: {And do not marry polytheist women until they believe} [al-Baqarah 2:221].
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage covers the prohibition of marrying women who worship idols (wathan) or any other objects of worship besides Allah, including stars and all other forms of polytheism. The ruling extends to all non-Muslim women who are not People of the Book. The primary evidence is the Qur'anic verse in Surah al-Baqarah (2:221) which explicitly prohibits marriage to polytheist women until they have faith (iman). The verse's generality includes all mushrikat, so only Muslim women or women from Ahl al-Kitab are permissible.

Prohibition of a Fifth Wife During a Fourth's 'Iddah
Ruling with brief reasoning and analogy to slaves

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the prohibition of marrying a fifth wife while a fourth is in 'iddah and apply the same logic to a slave's limit.

Core Idea
The 'iddah partially retains the marriage tie, so the limit of four wives remains until it expires.

This passage establishes a key ruling in Islamic marriage law: when a man has four wives and divorces one, he cannot immediately marry a fifth. The waiting period ('iddah) keeps the marriage partially intact, preventing him from exceeding the maximum of four. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and extends it to slaves.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is marrying a fifth wife while a fourth is in 'iddah. The ruling is haram (prohibited) for a free man.
The source explicitly states 'لا يحل' (not permissible).
2
Understand the condition triggering the prohibition
The man must have four wives at the time of divorce. The divorced wife is in 'iddah. The prohibition lasts until the 'iddah expires.
If he had fewer than four, this ruling does not apply.
3
Analyze the reasoning: partial retention of marriage
The author explains that during 'iddah, the marriage remains partially (من وجه). Thus, the count of wives still includes the divorcée, so taking a fifth would exceed the limit.
This is a key Hanafi principle: 'iddah has some effects of a valid marriage.
4
Apply the analogy to a slave
For a slave, the limit is two wives. So during the 'iddah of a second wife, he cannot marry a third. The same logic applies with adjusted numbers.
The source explicitly compares: 'وأما للعبد فلا يجوز الثالثة في عدة الثانية'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخامسة في عدة رابعة: هذا للحر، وأما للعبد فلا يجوز الثالثة في عدة الثانية. يعني إذا كانت له أربع زوجات فطلق إحداهن لا يحل له نكاح خامسة ما لم تنقض العدة؛ لأن النكاح باق في العدة من وجه، فلو نكح خامسة مع كون إحدى الأربع معتدة تلزم الزيادة على الأربع.
And a fifth (wife) in the 'iddah of a fourth: this is for the free man. As for the slave, a third (wife) in the 'iddah of a second is not permissible. Meaning, if he has four wives and divorces one of them, it is not permissible for him to marry a fifth until the 'iddah expires; because the marriage remains partially during the 'iddah. So if he marries a fifth while one of the four is in 'iddah, he would be exceeding the limit of four.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage rules on the situation where a man has four wives, divorces one, and wishes to marry a fifth. It is not permissible until the divorced wife's 'iddah (waiting period) has ended. The reasoning is that divorce does not immediately sever all marital ties; the marriage still has an effect during the 'iddah. Therefore, marrying a fifth while the fourth is in 'iddah would violate the limit of four wives. For a slave, the limit is two wives, so a third during the 'iddah of a second is similarly prohibited.

Key Terms
العدة (al-'iddah): The waiting period after divorce or death during which a woman cannot remarry.
الزيادة على الأربع (al-ziyadat 'ala al-arba'): Exceeding the limit of four wives, which is prohibited.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Immediately after the divorce pronouncement
B) After the 'iddah of the fourth wife ends
C) After three months regardless
D) He cannot marry a fifth even after 'iddah

Key Takeaway
A man cannot circumvent the polygamy limit by marrying during a divorcée's 'iddah because the marriage is considered partially intact.

Prohibition of Marrying an Amah During a Free Wife's 'Iddah
Ruling that marrying an amah during a free wife's 'iddah is invalid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on marrying a slave woman during a free wife's 'iddah and explain the reasoning based on the legal effect of 'iddah.

Core Idea
Marrying a slave woman during a free wife's 'iddah is invalid because 'iddah retains the legal effect of marriage.

This lesson addresses a specific prohibition in marriage: a man cannot marry a slave woman while his free wife is in 'iddah from a divorce. The passage explains that the 'iddah period continues to have legal effects similar to marriage, creating an impediment. Understanding this ruling highlights the protective rights given to free wives and the legal continuity of 'iddah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act in question is marrying a slave woman (amah) during the 'iddah of a free wife. The ruling is that it is prohibited (haram) and the marriage is invalid (batil).
The prohibition is specific to slave women; marrying a free woman during 'iddah has different rules.
2
Understand the legal principle: 'iddah has the effect of marriage
The text states 'because the 'iddah has the ruling of marriage' (لأن للعدة حكم النكاح). This means the waiting period retains some marital effects, including the prohibition on adding a slave co-wife.
This principle is a key legal fiction in Hanafi fiqh.
3
Apply the principle to the scenario
Since 'iddah is like marriage, marrying an amah during it is equivalent to marrying an amah while already married to a free woman, which is prohibited if the free wife does not consent (and she is assumed not to).
The free wife's right not to have a slave co-wife is protected.
4
Note the consequence and scope
The marriage contract with the amah during this period is invalid (batil) and must be annulled. The prohibition applies to any divorce that leaves a free wife in 'iddah, whether revocable or irrevocable.
After the 'iddah ends, the man may marry the amah if other conditions are met.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمة على حرة أو في عدتها. يعني إذا طلق حرة لا يجوز نكاحه بأمة ما لم تنقض عدتها؛ لأن للعدة حكم النكاح، فيلزم نكاح الأمة على الحرة، وهو ممنوع، كما مر.
And a slave woman (in marriage) upon a free woman or in her 'iddah. Meaning, if he divorces a free woman, it is not permissible for him to marry a slave woman until her 'iddah expires; because the 'iddah has the ruling of marriage. So marrying a slave woman would be (an addition) upon the free woman, which is prohibited, as previously mentioned.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage states that a man who divorces his free wife cannot marry a slave woman (amah) until the free woman's 'iddah ends. The 'iddah retains some marital effect, so marrying an amah would be akin to marrying a second wife while the first is still in the marriage bond, which is forbidden if the first wife is free. This is because a free woman has a right not to have a slave co-wife, and the 'iddah preserves that right.

Key Terms
الأمة (al-amah): A female slave.

Key Takeaway
The 'iddah period maintains the legal effect of marriage for the purpose of prohibiting marriage to a slave woman, preserving the free wife's rights.

Ruling: prohibition of marrying a pregnant captive from the enemy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on marrying a captive woman found pregnant from the enemy and identify the conditions that make it permissible.

Core Idea
A pregnant captive from the enemy cannot be married until she delivers — to ensure lineage clarity.

This lesson addresses a specific situation in the law of captives: what to do when a captured woman is already pregnant from her non-Muslim husband. The Hanafi school, consistent with its emphasis on protecting lineage, prohibits marriage until the pregnancy is resolved. We will examine the ruling, its rationale, and the conditions for its application.

Guided Path

Prohibition of Marrying One's Umm Walad
Ruling that a master cannot marry his umm walad pregnant from him.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling that a master cannot marry his umm walad and explain the reason based on childbirth creating a mahram relationship.

Core Idea
A master cannot marry his umm walad because childbirth creates a permanent prohibition akin to mahramiyya.

This lesson addresses a specific ruling in family law: the prohibition of a master marrying his umm walad. Even though the master has the right of ownership and may have had lawful intercourse, the birth of a child changes the legal status, making marriage impermissible. We will examine the textual evidence and the underlying reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
State the act being ruled upon (marriage of a master to his umm walad) and the hukm (haram, marriage is invalid).
The ruling is absolute; it applies whether the umm walad is pregnant or not.
2
Identify the condition
The woman must be an umm walad – a slave who has borne her master's child. The condition of pregnancy is mentioned but not essential.
The text specifies 'pregnant from her master' but the ruling applies even if she is not pregnant.
3
Grasp the expansion: the reason for prohibition
Understand why childbirth prohibits marriage: the child is free and related to the master, making the umm walad like a mahram (unmarriageable relative).
The source says: 'because she became prohibited by childbirth' – the birth creates a relationship of lineage.
4
Locate the source of the ruling
Recognize that the ruling is from al-Hidayah and al-Binayah, authoritative Hanafi texts.
The 'waw' is conjunctive, linking to previous discussion.
5
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Consider a concrete example: a man owns a slave-girl who bears his child. He cannot marry her even after freeing her; the prohibition persists.
Freeing her does not remove the mahramiyya.

Commentary
This passage states that a man cannot marry his own umm walad (a slave who bore his child). Even though intercourse with her was halal for him as her master, the birth of his child creates a relationship that permanently prohibits marriage. The child is free and related to him, so marrying the mother would be like marrying a close relative. This is in the context of a pregnant umm walad, but the ruling applies regardless of pregnancy.

Key Terms

بالإبضاع (bil-ibḍāʿ): By intercourse (in the context of being permissible for the master).

Prohibition of Marrying Fifth Wife During Iddah and Related Cases
Commentary on prohibited women due to iddah and lineage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings prohibiting marriage due to iddah and lineage conflicts, and apply these rules to scenarios involving pregnant captives and umm walad.

Core Idea
Iddah preserves marital effects in Hanafi law, so a fifth wife cannot be added during iddah of a fourth.

This passage from the commentary on al-Hidayah addresses several prohibited marriage scenarios. The common thread is protecting lineage and respecting the limits set by Shariah. We explore rulings on idol-worshipers, iddah, captive pregnancy, and the unique case of umm walad.

Guided Path
1
Identify prohibited categories
List each type of woman mentioned: idol-worshipper, fifth during iddah of fourth, slave in iddah of free, pregnant captive, and pregnant umm walad.
2
Extract ruling and dalil

والوثنية، وخامسة في عدة رابعة وأمةٍ على حرّة، أو في عدتها، وحامل من سَبي، وحامل ثبت نسب حملها، ولو هي أم ولدٍ حَمَلَت من سيدها.
وعلى تقدير صحته نقول: لا نعني بالمجوس إلا عبدة النار، وكونهم كان معهم كتاب لا يفيد شيئاً، فإنه لا شك أنهم الآن داخلون في المشركين، وأيضاً بالرفع والنسيان قد أخرجوا من زمرة أهل الكتاب. ويشهد له ما أخرجه ابن أبي شيبة وعبد الرزاق عن الحسن بن محمد بن علي مرسلاً مرفوعاً: «إن النبي كتب إلى مجوس نجران من أسلم قبل منه، ومَن لم يُسْلِم ضربت عليه الجزية غير ناكحي نسائهم ولا آكلي ذبائحهم».
[١] قوله والوثنية: نسبة إلى وَثَن: وهو بفتحتين ماله جنّة: أي صورة من خشب أو حجر، والمراد بها عابدة الصنم، وكذا الحكمُ في عُبَّاد النجوم وغيرها من الأشياء وكل كافر، والأصل فيه قوله: {وَلَا تَنكِحُوا الْمُشْرِكَتِ حَتَّى يُؤْمِنَ} الآية.
[٢] قوله: وخامسة... الخ: يعني إذا كانت له أربع زوجات فطلق إحداها لا يحلُّ له نكاح خامسة ما لم تنقض العدّة؛ لأن النكاح باق في العدة من وجه، فلو نكح خامسة مع كون إحدى الأربع معتدة تلزم الزيادة على الأربع.
[٣] قوله: أو في عدتها: يعني إذا طلق حرَّةً لا يجوز نكاحه بأمة ما لم تنقض عدتها؛ لأن للعدّة حكم النكاح، فيلزم نكاح الأمة على الحرة، وهو ممنوع، كما مرّ.
[٤] قوله: من سبي: - بالفتح -: أي لا يجوز نكاح امرأة سبيت، وهي حاملة من حربي، وكذا المهاجرة إلينا، وهي حاملة من حربي.
[٥] قوله ولو هي أم ولد: الواو واصلية، قال في «الهداية» وشرحها «البناية»: إن تزوج مسبية سبية حاملاً لا يجوزُ النكاح؛ لأنَّ حملها ثابتُ النَّسب، وإنما أفردها بالذكر، وإن كانت داخلة تحت قوله: وحامل ثبت نسب حملها؛ لأنه قد يُشتَبَهُ أَنَّ ولدها ثابت النسب أم لا، فلا يعلم حكم نكاحها، فأفردَها بالذكر.

تزوج أم ولده، وهي حامل منه: أي من المولى، فالنكاح باطل؛ لأنها فراش لمولاها؛ لوجود حده وهو صيرورة المرأة متينة؛ لثبوت نسب الولد منها، وهو معنى قوله: «حتى يثبت نسب ولدها منه من غير دعوة». فلو صح النكاح الحصل الجمع بين الفراشين، وهما فراش المولى وفراش الناكح، وأنه لا يجوز؛ لأنه يؤدّي إلى اشتباه الأنساب كإنكاح المنكوحة إلا أنه غير متأكد، ويجوز أن يكون جواباً عما يقال: لو كانت فراشاً لمولاها لما جازَ نكاحها عند كونها غير حامل مع أنه يجوز أي إلا أن فراشها غير متأكد يعني ضعيف حتى ينتفي الولد بالنفي من غير لعان، فلا يعتبر ما لم يتصل به الحمل؛ لأن الحمل مانع في الجملة، وكذلك الفراش فعند اجتماعهما يحصل التأكيد.

فإن قلت: إذا كان غير متأكد ينتفي الولد من غير لعان وجب أن يكون الإقدام على النكاح نفياً للسبب، فإنه يقبلُ النفي دلالة كما إذا قالت الجارية له: ولدت ثلاثة أولاد في بطون مختلفة، هذا الأكبر منّي، فإنه منتفي نسب الباقين، وإذا انتفى نسبه كان حملا غير ثابت النسب، وفي مثله يجوز النكاح.
قلت: أجيب بأن هذا دلالة، والدلالة إنما تعمل إذا لم يخالفها صريح، والصريح هاهنا موجود؛ لأن المسألة فيما إذا كان الحمل منه.

[١] قوله: مسبية؛ على وزن مرميّة: أي المرأة التي سبيت وأخذت من أيدي الكفار.
[٢] قوله: وإنما أفردها... الخ: دفع لما يقال: إنه لم يكن حاجة إلى قوله: وحامل لكفاية قوله: «وحامل ثبت نسب حملها»، فإن الحامل المسبية نسب حملها ثابت، فلا يجوز نكاحها، وحاصل الدفع أنه إنما ذكر هذه الصورة على حدة؛ لأنها كانت مظنة أن نسب ولد المسبية غير ثابت.
And the idol-worshipping woman, and a fifth in the iddah of a fourth, and a slave woman upon a free woman, or in her iddah, and a pregnant woman from captivity, and a pregnant woman whose pregnancy's lineage is established, even if she is an umm walad who became pregnant from her master. And on the assumption of its correctness, we say: We mean by Magians only fire-worshippers, and that they once had a book does not benefit anything, for there is no doubt that they are now included among the polytheists. Also, by abrogation and forgetfulness, they have been removed from the group of the People of the Book. This is supported by what Ibn Abi Shaybah and Abd al-Razzaq narrated from al-Hasan ibn Muhammad ibn Ali as a mursal marfu' hadith: 'The Prophet wrote to the Magians of Najran: whoever accepts Islam is accepted from him, and whoever does not accept Islam, jizyah is imposed upon him, and you shall not marry their women nor eat their slaughtered animals.' [1] His saying 'and the idol-worshipping woman': a relative attribution to 'wathan', which is with two fathas, a thing having a form: a figure of wood or stone, and by it is meant the worshipper of the idol. The same ruling applies to worshippers of stars and other things, and every disbeliever. The basis for this is His saying: 'And do not marry polytheistic women until they believe.' [2] His saying 'and a fifth...etc.': meaning if he has four wives and divorces one of them, it is not lawful for him to marry a fifth until her iddah expires, because the marriage remains in iddah in some respect. So if he marries a fifth while one of the four is in iddah, an increase beyond four necessarily occurs. [3] His saying 'or in her iddah': meaning if he divorces a free woman, it is not permissible for him to marry a slave woman until her iddah expires, because iddah has the ruling of marriage, so marrying a slave woman upon the free woman would result in a prohibited combination, as previously mentioned. [4] His saying 'from captivity': with fatha: meaning it is not permissible to marry a woman who has been taken captive while she is pregnant from a harbi, and likewise a woman who has migrated to us while pregnant from a harbi. [5] His saying 'even if she is an umm walad': the waw is waw al-silah. It is stated in al-Hidayah and its commentary al-Binayah: If one marries a captive woman (sabiyyah) who is pregnant, the marriage is not valid because her pregnancy's lineage is established. And he mentioned it separately even though it is included under his saying 'and a pregnant woman whose pregnancy's lineage is established' because it might be ambiguous whether her child's lineage is established or not, so one would not know the ruling of marrying her; thus he mentioned it separately. And if one marries his umm walad while she is pregnant from him (the master), the marriage is invalid because she is a bedding (firash) for her master due to the existence of its condition, which is the woman becoming 'matinah' due to the establishment of the child's lineage from him, and this is the meaning of his saying 'until the child's lineage from him is established without a claim.' So if the marriage were valid, it would result in combining two beddings: the master's bedding and the husband's bedding, and that is not permissible because it leads to confusion of lineages, like marrying a woman who is already married, except that it is less certain. And it may be a response to what is said: if she were a bedding for her master, it would not be permissible to marry her when she is not pregnant, yet it is permissible. That is because her bedding is less certain (weak) such that the child is negated by denial without li'an, so it is not considered unless it is connected to pregnancy, because pregnancy is a preventive in some sense, and similarly the bedding. So when both combine, certainty is achieved. If you say: if it is less certain and the child is negated without li'an, then proceeding with marriage should be a denial of the cause, for denial is accepted by indication, as when a slave girl says to him: 'I gave birth to three children in different wombs, this eldest is from me,' and the lineage of the remaining is negated. When their lineage is negated, it becomes a pregnancy with unestablished lineage, and in such a case marriage is permissible. I say: The answer is that this is indication, and indication only operates if it is not contradicted by an explicit statement, and the explicit statement is present here, because the matter is where the pregnancy is from him. [1] His saying 'captive woman' (masebiyyah): on the pattern of marmeeyyah: meaning the woman who was captured and taken from the hands of the disbelievers. [2] His saying 'and he mentioned it separately...etc.': a repelling of the objection that there was no need for his saying 'and a pregnant woman' since his saying 'and a pregnant woman whose pregnancy's lineage is established' sufficed, for the captive pregnant woman's pregnancy's lineage is established, so it is not permissible to marry her. The summary of the repelling is that he mentioned this case separately because it was a place of assumption that the lineage of the captive woman's child is not established.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a set of rulings from the matn regarding prohibited women in marriage. The commentator begins by listing several categories: idol-worshipping women (the commentator later clarifies that Magians are included in polytheists, not People of the Book, citing a hadith that prohibits marrying their women and eating their slaughtered animals), marrying a fifth wife while a fourth is in iddah (because the marriage is considered ongoing in iddah, so it would exceed the limit of four), marrying a slave woman while a free wife is in iddah (as it combines a slave with a free woman in a prohibited manner), marrying a captive woman who is pregnant from a harbi (because the child's lineage is established from the father, so the marriage would confuse lineage), and marrying an umm walad (a slave mother of a child) who is pregnant from her master (since she is a bedding for her master, marrying her would combine two beddings and cause lineage confusion). The commentator discusses the concept of firash (bedding) and its strength, explaining that the bedding of an umm walad is weak until pregnancy occurs, after which it becomes strong. He also addresses a potential objection regarding the permissibility of marrying an umm walad when not pregnant, clarifying that the prohibition is specific to pregnancy. The commentary relies on Quranic verses (2:221) and hadith (from Ibn Abi Shaybah) as proofs.

Key Terms
عدة (iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of husband before she can remarry.
سبي (saby): Captivity; a woman taken captive from war (harbi) with a child from the enemy.
أم ولد (umm walad): A female slave who has given birth to a child from her master, thereby gaining certain rights and becoming a 'mother of the child'.
وثنية (wathaniyyah): Idol-worshipping woman; one who worships a wathan (idol).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because iddah is a period of mourning and marriage during it is disrespectful
B) Because the marriage is considered ongoing in some respect during iddah, so marrying a fifth would exceed the limit of four
C) Because the wife in iddah might be pregnant and marriage would cause lineage confusion
D) Because the husband must wait until the iddah of all divorced wives is complete

Key Takeaway
Marriage prohibitions in iddah and pregnancy cases protect lineage and uphold the limit of four wives.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
8 min
Marriage to Pregnant Captive or Umm Walad: Invalid to Preserve Lineage
Explaining the invalidity of marriage when pregnancy is established

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on marrying a pregnant woman with established lineage and explain the qiyas basis using the illa of lineage confusion.

Core Idea
Marriage to a woman whose pregnancy is from a known father is invalid to avoid lineage confusion.

This lesson examines a key principle in Hanafi family law: the invalidity of marrying a pregnant woman when the child's paternity is fixed. The commentary explains why this applies even to a weak marital bed (firash), using analogy to marrying a married woman. We will unpack the qiyas structure, the hypothetical objection, and the classification of firash strengths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
The act is marrying a pregnant captive (mabsiyyah) or a pregnant umm walad. The ruling is that the marriage is invalid (batil). State this clearly.
2
Locate the Dalil (Proof)
The proof is not direct text but qiyas (analogy) to the prohibition of marrying a married woman (mankuhah). Identify the original case (asl) and the new case (far').
The asl is a married woman, the far' is a pregnant woman with established lineage from another man.
3
Trace the Wajh al-Dalalah (Effective Cause)
The illa (effective cause) is confusion of lineages (ishtibah al-ansab). Confirm that this cause is present in both cases. In the far', pregnancy from a known father creates a firash that would be shared if marriage occurs.
The passage mentions that pregnancy strengthens the firash, making the cause explicit (jaliyyah).
4
Note the Objection and Response
A hypothetical objection: if the umm walad's firash is weak (the child can be repudiated by denial without li'an), why is marriage invalid? The response: pregnancy provides an explicit reality (sarih) that overrides implied denial (dalalah). Thus the firash becomes strong.
This shows how the strength of firash can be modified by the fact of pregnancy.
5
Understand the Firash Hierarchy
The passage outlines four ranks of firash: weak (regular slave), medium (umm walad), strong (married woman), strongest (irrevocably divorced). Recognize that the ruling in our case applies even to the medium rank because of pregnancy.
This classification helps explain the spectrum of lineage certainty.

Source Text

تزوج مسبية سبية حاملاً لا يجوزُ النكاح ؛ لأنَّ حملها ثابتُ النَّسب ، وإنما أفردها" بالذكر، وإن كانت داخلة تحت قوله : وحامل ثبت نسب حملها ؛ لأنه قد يُشتَبَهُ أَنَّ ولدها ثابت النسب أم لا ، فلا يعلم حكم نكاحها، فأفردَها بالذكر. تزوج أم ولده، وهي حامل منه : أي من المولى، فالنكاح باطل ؛ لأنها فراش لمولاها ؛ لوجود حده وهو صيرورة المرأة متينة ؛ لثبوت نسب الولد منها ، وهو معنى قوله : «حتى يثبت نسب ولدها منه من غير دعوة» فلو صح النكاح الحصل الجمع بين الفراشين، وهما فراش المولى وفراش الناكح ، وأنه لا يجوز؛ لأنه يؤدّي إلى اشتباه الأنساب كإنكاح المنكوحة إلا أنه غير متأكد، ويجوز أن يكون جواباً عما يقال : لو كانت فراشاً لمولاها لما جازَ نكاحها عند كونها غير حامل مع أنه يجوز أي إلا أن فراشها غير متأكد يعني ضعيف حتى ينتفي الولد بالنفي من غير لعان، فلا يعتبر ما لم يتصل به الحمل ؛ لأن الحمل مانع في الجملة، وكذلك الفراش فعند اجتماعهما يحصل التأكيد. فإن قلت : إذا كان غير متأكد ينتفي الولد من غير لعان وجب أن يكون الإقدام على النكاح نفياً للسبب، فإنه يقبلُ النفي دلالة كما إذا قالت الجارية له : ولدت ثلاثة أولاد في بطون مختلفة، هذا الأكبر منّي ، فإنه منتفي نسب الباقين، وإذا انتفى نسبه كان حملا غير ثابت النسب، وفي مثله يجوز النكاح. [٢] قوله : هي على أربع مراتب : (1) الفراش القوي ؛ ذكر في «الدر المختار» ) وحواشيه : إن الفراش ١ .ضعيف : وهـو فـراش الأمة لا يثبت النسب فيه إلا بالدعوة بكسر الدال : أي دعوى الولد. م ٢. ومتوسط : وهو فراش أمّ الولد ، فإنّه يثبتُ فيه بلا دعوة، لكنه ينتفي بالنفي. ٣. وقوي : وهو فراش المنكوحة، ومعتدة الرجعي، فإنه فيه لا ينتفي إلا باللعان. ٤. وأقوى : كفراش معتدة البائن، فإن الولد لا ينتفى فيه أصلاً ؛ لأن نفيه متوقف على اللعان، وشرط اللعان الزوجية. (۳) قوله : وأيضاً ... الخ ؛ وجه آخر لقوله : «ولو هي أم ولد»، وحاصله : أنه قد ذكر المصنف له سابقاً أن نكاح موطوءة السيد صحيح ، وأنه لا يجب فيه على زوجها الاستبراء، فكان مظنة أن يتوهم متوهم صحة نكاح الحاملة من السيد أيضاً لصدق موطوءة السيد عليها، فلدفع هذا الوَهَم صرَّحَ بحكمها بقوله : «ولو هي أم ولد». If one marries a captured female slave who is pregnant, the marriage is not permissible because her pregnancy has established lineage. And he only mentioned her specifically, even though she is included under his statement: 'a pregnant woman whose pregnancy's lineage is established,' because it might be doubtful whether her child's lineage is established or not, so the ruling of her marriage is unknown, so he mentioned her specifically. If he marries his umm walad while she is pregnant from him—meaning from the master—the marriage is invalid because she is a marital bed (firash) for her master due to the presence of its condition, which is the woman becoming firm (matinah) due to the establishment of the child's lineage from her, and that is the meaning of his statement: 'until the lineage of her child from him is established without a claim.' So if the marriage were valid, it would combine two marital beds: the master's marital bed and the husband's marital bed, and that is not permissible because it leads to confusion of lineages, like marrying a married woman, except that it is not strong. And it may be a response to what is said: if she were a marital bed for her master, then her marriage would not be permissible when she is not pregnant, despite being permissible—meaning except that her marital bed is not strong, i.e., weak, so that the child is repudiated by denial without li'an, so it is not considered unless pregnancy is connected to it because pregnancy is a preventative in general, and likewise the marital bed, so when they combine, strength occurs. If you say: if it is not strong, the child is repudiated by denial without li'an, then proceeding with the marriage must be a denial of the cause, for denial is accepted by implication, just as if the slave girl says to him: I gave birth to three children in different pregnancies, this oldest is from me, then the lineage of the remaining ones is negated, and if his lineage is negated, it becomes a pregnancy with unestablished lineage, and in such a case marriage is permissible. (1) I say: the response is that this is by implication, and implication only operates when it does not contradict an explicit statement, and the explicit statement is present here because the case is when the pregnancy is from him. [1] The author's statement: 'mabsiyyah' (captured): on the weight of marmiyyah: meaning the woman who is captured and taken from the hands of the disbelievers, from captivity. [2] His statement: 'and he only mentioned her specially...' etc.: a rebuttal to what is said: there was no need for his statement 'and a pregnant woman' because his statement 'and a pregnant woman whose pregnancy's lineage is established' suffices, for the pregnant captured woman's pregnancy's lineage is established, so her marriage is not permissible. The summary of the rebuttal is that he mentioned this case separately because it was a likely case that the lineage of the captured woman's child was not established. (1) End from Al-Binayah Sharh Al-Hidayah, 94-95, abbreviated. 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah 'ala Sharh al-Wiqayah. And his statement: 'and if she is an umm walad'; and he said thus—and such speech is used in a context needing emphasis—because the pregnant woman whose pregnancy's lineage is established is either a married woman (mankuhah) or a mustawlidah. The married woman has a strong marital bed. So to reject the misconception that this ruling is specific to the strong marital bed, he said: 'the marriage of a pregnant woman whose pregnancy's lineage is established is invalid, even if the marital bed is not strong.' And also, it has been mentioned that the marriage of a female slave who is regularly copulated with by the master is valid, so this meaning might give the impression of validity [1] His statement: 'because the pregnant woman...' etc.: its summary: the pregnant woman whose pregnancy's lineage is established from someone is either married to another or a mustawlidah: she who is made an umm walad, and she is the slave girl whom her master copulated with and claimed her child, and the married woman's marital bed is strong. So to reject the misconception that the invalidity of marriage is specific to the strong marital bed, he also stated the case of the weak marital bed. [2] His statement: 'It is of four ranks: (1) the marital bed; mentioned in Al-Durr al-Mukhtar and its commentaries: the marital bed. 1. Weak: which is the marital bed of a regular slave girl; lineage is not established in it except by a claim (da'wah, with a kasra on the dal): meaning a claim of the child. 2. Medium: which is the marital bed of the umm walad; lineage is established in it without a claim, but it is repudiated by denial. 3. Strong: which is the marital bed of the married woman and the woman in a revocable divorce; lineage is not repudiated in it except by li'an. 4. Strongest: like the marital bed of the woman in an irrevocable divorce; the child is never repudiated because its denial depends on li'an, and the condition for li'an is marriage. (3) His statement: 'and also...' etc.: another aspect for his statement: 'and if she is an umm walad'; its summary: the author earlier mentioned that the marriage of a female slave copulated with by the master is valid, and that there is no obligation of istibra' on her husband, so there was a likelihood that someone might mistakenly assume the validity of marrying the pregnant woman from the master as well, because 'female slave copulated with by the master' applies to her. So to reject this misconception, he clarified her ruling with his statement: 'and if she is an umm walad'. (1) Al-Durr al-Mukhtar and Radd al-Muhtar, 3:550.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on a matn from Al-Wiqayah, explaining the ruling on marrying a pregnant woman whose pregnancy's lineage is established. The commentator addresses two cases: (1) a captured slave woman (mabsiyyah) who is pregnant from her previous master; (2) one's own umm walad (mother of one's child) who is pregnant from the master. In both cases, the marriage is invalid (la yajuz/batil). The reasoning is that the pregnancy establishes the child's lineage to the biological father (the master in both cases). To avoid confusion of lineages (ishtibah al-ansab), marrying such a woman is prohibited by analogy (qiyas) to marrying a woman who is already married (mankuhah). The commentary then addresses a potential objection: if the marital bed (firash) of the umm walad is weak (ghayr muta'akkid) because the child can be repudiated by simple denial without li'an, then why is the marriage invalid? The response is that pregnancy itself strengthens the marital bed, and the explicit fact of pregnancy (the visible state) overrides any implied denial. The passage also discusses the ranks of marital bed (firash) from weak to strongest, illustrating degrees of lineage establishment. The commentator emphasizes that the author mentioned these specific cases to remove misconceptions: for the captured woman, because one might think her child's lineage is unclear; for the umm walad, because one might think her weak firash allows marriage. The overall message is that once pregnancy is established with a known father, marriage to her is invalid to preserve lineage integrity.

Key Terms
مسبية (mabsiyyah): A female captive taken from the disbelievers
أم ولد (umm walad): A slave woman who bears a child to her master, and the master acknowledges the child as his own
فراش (firash): The marital bed or the relationship through which paternity is established

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is marriage to a pregnant umm walad invalid according to the Hanafi school?
A) Because she is still a slave
B) Because it would combine two marital beds leading to lineage confusion
C) Because she is in iddah
D) Because the master has not given permission

Key Takeaway
Marriage to a pregnant woman whose pregnancy is from a known father is invalid to avoid confusion of lineages, regardless of the inherent strength of the marital bed.

The Invalidity of Marriage to a Pregnant Umm Walad
Commentary explaining the invalidity of marriage for a pregnant woman even if she is an umm walad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on marrying a pregnant woman with established lineage and explain why the ruling applies even to an umm walad by analyzing the ranks of firash.

Core Idea
Marrying a pregnant woman with established lineage is batil to prevent lineage confusion, regardless of her status.

This lesson unpacks a classic Hanafi ruling from the Wiqayah: the marriage of a pregnant woman whose pregnancy is attributable to another man is invalid. The commentator, 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah, clarifies that this applies even if she is an umm walad (a slave mother), dismissing any doubt that the ruling only covers free wives. Understanding the four ranks of firash (marital bed) helps explain why lineage protection overrides other considerations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key ruling
The base text (matn) states: 'The marriage of a pregnant woman whose pregnancy lineage is established is invalid (batil), even if she is an umm walad.' Recognize this as the core ruling.
The ruling is unconditional once the pregnancy's paternity is fixed.
2
Recognize the commentator's expansion
The commentator provides two reasons for the author's inclusion of 'even if she is an umm walad': first, to remove the suspicion that the ruling only applies to strong firash (free wives); second, to prevent the misunderstanding that because the marriage of a master's concubine is valid, a pregnant umm walad's marriage might also be valid.
The expansion clarifies the scope of the ruling.
3
Analyze the four ranks of firash
Learn the hierarchy of firash in Hanafi fiqh: weak (slave woman), medium (umm walad), strong (free wife and woman in revocable divorce), and strongest (woman in irrevocable divorce). Each has different rules for lineage establishment and negation.
The umm walad's firash is medium – lineage is established without claim but can be negated by denial.
4
Apply to the umm walad case
Since the umm walad's child's lineage is already established (by the master's acknowledgment), her marriage to another would cause lineage confusion. Therefore, her marriage is batil even though her firash is only medium.
The strength of firash is irrelevant once lineage is fixed.
5
Understand the second reason
The commentator also notes that the author earlier stated that the marriage of a master's concubine (mawtua al-sayyid) is valid. To prevent the assumption that a pregnant umm walad (who is also a concubine) could be validly married, the author explicitly includes her in the ruling.
This shows the author's thoroughness in excluding all possible exceptions.

Source Text

(۳) قوله : وأيضاً ... الخ ؛ وجه آخر لقوله : «ولو هي أم ولد»، وحاصله : أنه قد ذكر المصنف له سابقاً أن نكاح موطوءة السيد صحيح ، وأنه لا يجب فيه على زوجها الاستبراء، فكان مظنة أن يتوهم متوهم صحة نكاح الحاملة من السيد أيضاً لصدق موطوءة السيد عليها، فلدفع هذا الوهم صرح بحكمها بقوله : «ولو هي أم ولد». نكاح الحامل من السيد، فإنها موطوءة السيد ، فقال : بطل نكاح حامل ثبت نسب حملها، وإن كانت هذه الحامل موطوءة السيد، فإنَّ هذا المعنى يوجب صحة النكاح ، فمع ذلك بطل نكاحها باعتبار ثبوتِ نسب حملها.
His statement: 'And even if she is an umm walad.' He said that only because this type of speech is used in a context needing emphasis, because the pregnant woman whose pregnancy lineage is established is either a married free woman or a milk-mother (mustawlada). The married free woman is the strong firash (bed). To repel the suspicion that this ruling is specific to the strong firash, he said: 'The marriage of a pregnant woman whose pregnancy lineage is established is invalid, even if the firash is not strong.' Also, he mentioned that the marriage of the master's concubine (mawtua al-sayyid) is valid, so this meaning might suggest the validity of marrying a pregnant woman from the master. [1] His statement: 'Because the pregnant woman...' etc.; its summary: The pregnant woman whose pregnancy lineage is established is either another's married free woman or a mustawlada, i.e., one made an umm walad, a female slave whom her master has intercourse with and claims her child. The firash of the married free woman is strong. To repel the suspicion that the invalidity of the marriage is specific to the strong firash, he clarified the case of the weak firash as well. [2] His statement: 'It is of four ranks': (1) The strong firash; mentioned in al-Durr al-Mukhtar and its glosses: The firash: 1. Weak: the firash of a slave woman, lineage is only established by claim (da'wa). 2. Medium: the firash of an umm walad, lineage is established without claim but is negated by denial. 3. Strong: the firash of a married free woman and a woman in revocable divorce, lineage is not negated except by li'an. 4. Strongest: like the firash of a woman in irrevocable divorce, the child is never negated because its negation depends on li'an and the condition of li'an is marriage. (3) His statement: 'Also...' etc.; another aspect of his statement 'And even if she is an umm walad'; its summary: The author previously mentioned that the marriage of the master's concubine is valid and that her husband is not required to observe istibra', so it became a place where one might suspect the validity of marrying a pregnant woman from the master as well because the term 'master's concubine' applies to her. To repel this suspicion, he clarified her ruling by saying: 'And even if she is an umm walad.' The marriage of a pregnant woman from the master, since she is the master's concubine, he said: 'The marriage of a pregnant woman whose pregnancy lineage is established is invalid, even if this pregnant woman is the master's concubine,' because this meaning would suggest the validity of the marriage, yet her marriage is invalid due to the established lineage of her pregnancy.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the author's statement in the matn about the invalidity of marrying a pregnant woman. The commentator explains why the author specifically said 'even if she is an umm walad' (a slave woman who has borne her master a child). The reason is to remove any misconception that the ruling only applies to free married women (strong firash). The commentator then elaborates on the four ranks of firash (marital bed) in Hanafi fiqh: weak (slave woman), medium (umm walad), strong (free wife and woman in revocable divorce), and strongest (woman in irrevocable divorce). Each rank has different rules regarding the establishment and negation of lineage. The commentator also gives a second reason for the author's phrasing: to prevent the misunderstanding that because the marriage of a master's concubine is valid, the marriage of a pregnant woman who is a master's concubine might also be valid. The clear ruling is that such a marriage is invalid (batil) because the paternity of the child is already established from another man, leading to confusion of lineage.

Key Terms
أم ولد (umm walad): A female slave who has borne a child to her master, and the master has acknowledged the child; she becomes free upon the master's death.
الفراش (al-firash): The marital relationship or bed upon which lineage is established; it has four ranks in Hanafi fiqh: weak, medium, strong, and strongest.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on marrying a pregnant woman who is an umm walad?
A) The marriage is valid because she is a master's concubine.
B) The marriage is invalid (batil) because her pregnancy lineage is established.
C) The marriage is valid only if the master gives permission.
D) The marriage is invalid only if she is a free married woman.

Key Takeaway
The marriage of a pregnant woman with established lineage is invalid in Hanafi fiqh to prevent confusion of lineage, regardless of the woman's status (free wife or umm walad).

The Permanent Prohibition of Nikah al-Mut'ah
Explicit prohibition of mut'ah with multiple hadith evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on nikah al-mut'ah and explain the hadith evidence for its permanent prohibition.

Core Idea
Nikah al-mut'ah is haram and invalid — permanently abrogated by clear prophetic prohibition.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah presents the definitive Hanafi stance on temporary marriage. The author traces the historical sequence of permission and prohibition, culminating in a final, irreversible ban, and supports it with multiple hadith from Sahih al-Bukhari and Sahih Muslim. Understanding this ruling requires appreciating the weight of the prophetic evidence and the principle that later abrogation overrides earlier permissions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Nikah al-mut'ah is a temporary marriage for a fixed period in exchange for dower. The author states it is not permissible (la yajuz), meaning it is haram.
The ruling is absolute prohibition; the contract is batil if performed.
2
Trace the historical sequence
The text notes that mut'ah was initially permitted in early Islam, then prohibited, then permitted again, and finally prohibited permanently. This sequence shows that the final prohibition abrogated all previous permissions.
Abrogation (naskh) is a key principle here.
3
Examine the hadith evidence
Multiple hadith are cited: prohibition at Khaybar (7 AH), prohibition at the Conquest of Makkah (8 AH), and a general prohibition until the Day of Resurrection. Each hadith is from authentic collections (Bukhari, Muslim).
The hadith of 'Ali and Samurah provide explicit prohibitions.
4
Understand the legal implication
The explicit prophetic prohibition (nahy) indicates haram. The commentator concludes that after such clear evidence, no one can claim permissibility.
The Hanafi position is that mut'ah is absolutely haram and invalid.
5
Note the cross-madhhab ikhtilaf
While the Hanafi and other Sunni schools hold it haram, some Shi'a schools consider it valid. However, the Hanafi ruling is based on sound dalil and is the mufta bihi.
This ikhtilaf is legitimate but the Hanafi position is firmly established.

And temporary marriage. [1] His statement: 'And temporary marriage' means that temporary marriage is not permissible. It was permissible in the beginning of Islam, then he prohibited it, then he permitted it, then he prohibited it. Al-Bukhari and Muslim narrated from Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) that the Prophet (peace be upon him) prohibited the enjoyment (mut'ah) of women on the day of Khaybar in the year seven Hijri, and also the flesh of domesticated donkeys, and that was the expedition of Khaybar. Muslim narrated from Samurah (may Allah be pleased with him) who said: 'The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) permitted us temporary marriage, then he prohibited it at the conquest of Makkah in the year eight.' In another wording from him: 'He went on an expedition with the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) and he commanded us to do temporary marriage during the conquest when we entered Makkah, then he did not leave it (Makkah) until he prohibited it.' In a narration: 'The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: O people, I had permitted you to enjoy women, but indeed Allah has forbidden that until the Day of Resurrection. So whoever has any of them with him, let him release her, and do not take anything of what you have given them.' In a narration by Muslim: 'The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) permitted temporary marriage in the year of Awtas for three days, then he prohibited it.' The year of the expedition of Awtas and the conquest of Makkah are the same. There are many narrations on this topic in the authentic collections and musnads, and after them there is no proof for anyone to permit it, whoever he may be. Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is an authoritative statement on the prohibition of nikah al-mut'ah (temporary marriage) with extensive hadith evidence. The commentator begins by stating that mut'ah is not permissible. He notes its historical permissibility in early Islam, followed by a sequence of prohibition and permission, culminating in a final permanent prohibition. The commentator then cites multiple authentic hadith: the prohibition at Khaybar (7 AH), the prohibition at the conquest of Makkah (8 AH), and the explicit statement that Allah has forbidden it until the Day of Resurrection. He also mentions the report from Ali rebuking Ibn Abbas for laxity on this issue. The commentator concludes that after such clear evidence, no one has any justification to permit it. This is the established Hanafi position: mut'ah is haram (forbidden) and the ruling is final.

Key Terms
نكاح المتعة (nikah al-mut'ah): A temporary marriage contract for a fixed period in exchange for a dower, which was initially permitted in early Islam but later permanently prohibited.
الاستمتاع (al-istimta'): Enjoyment or benefit, here referring to temporary marriage (mut'ah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) It is recommended only under certain conditions.
D) It is disliked (makruh) but not invalid.

Key Takeaway
Nikah al-mut'ah is haram (forbidden) in the Hanafi school, permanently abrogated by multiple authentic hadith that clearly prohibit it.
