Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
10 min
Prohibition of Mut'ah Marriage and Its Proofs
Declares mut'ah marriage prohibited with multiple hadith proofs and distinguishes mu'qqat as a form of mut'ah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on mut'ah marriage, cite the key hadith proofs, and explain why any time-limited marriage (mu'qqat) is considered a form of mut'ah and thus prohibited.

Core Idea
Mut'ah is permanently prohibited; any marriage with a fixed term, regardless of wording, is a form of mut'ah and invalid.

This lesson examines the ruling on mut'ah (temporary marriage). While it was permitted in early Islam, it was permanently forbidden by the Prophet ﷺ. We will study the hadith proofs and understand why the Hanafi school considers even a marriage contracted with permanent wording but an implicit time limit as prohibited.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Mut'ah marriage is declared not permissible (haram). The passage immediately states لا يجوز.
Ruling: haram.
2
Trace the chronological history
The passage notes that mut'ah was permitted, then forbidden, then permitted again, then finally forbidden permanently.
This indicates the final abrogation.
3
Locate the hadith proofs
Multiple hadith from Bukhari and Muslim are cited: prohibition at Khaybar (7 AH), permission and then prohibition at the conquest of Makkah (8 AH), and a narration stating Allah has forbidden it until the Day of Judgment.
Explicit prohibition (نهى) indicates haram.
4
Distinguish mut'ah from mu'qqat
The commentary from al-Fath explains that mu'qqat (time-limited marriage using nikah wording) is essentially the same as mut'ah because the contract is for a fixed term, despite having witnesses and standard wording.
The key is the temporary intent, not the wording.
5
Understand Zufar's view and the Hanafi position
Zufar considered mu'qqat to be permanent with the time condition void. The Hanafi position (mufta bihi) rejects this, holding that mu'qqat is a form of mut'ah and thus invalid.
The relied-upon stance is prohibition.

And mut'ah marriage: meaning mut'ah marriage is not permissible. It was permissible at the beginning of Islam, then he forbade it, then allowed it, then forbade it. Al-Bukhari and Muslim reported from Ali that the Prophet forbade mut'ah with women on the day of Khaybar in the year seven of Hijrah, and forbade the meat of domestic donkeys. And Muslim reported from Samurah that he said: The Messenger of Allah allowed us mut'ah, then forbade it at the conquest of Makkah, which was in the year eight. And in another wording: He fought with the Messenger of Allah and he commanded us to do mut'ah at the conquest of Makkah when we entered Makkah, then he did not leave it until he forbade it. And in a narration: The Messenger of Allah said: O people, I had permitted you to enjoy women, but Allah has forbidden that until the Day of Judgment. So whoever has any of them with him, let him release her, and do not take anything from what you have given them. And in a narration of Muslim: The Messenger of Allah allowed mut'ah in the year of Awtas for three days, then forbade it. And the year of the battle of Awtas and the conquest of Makkah is one. And there are many narrations in the sahih and musnad collections, and after them there is no proof for its permissibility for anyone. Then he said in al-Fath: The Shaykh al-Islam said regarding the difference between mut'ah marriage and time-limited marriage: The time-limited marriage is mentioned with the wording of nikah and tazwij, while mut'ah is 'I enjoy you' or 'I seek enjoyment', meaning what includes the root of mut'ah. And what appears with that is the absence of condition of witnesses and specification of duration in mut'ah, while in time-limited marriage witnesses and specification are required. And there is no doubt that they have no evidence for specifying that the mut'ah which was permitted then forbidden is what combines the root (m t '), based on the conclusive reports that the permission was for mut'ah. And it does not mean that whoever engages in this addresses her with the wording 'I enjoy' and the like, because it is known that the word is used and its meaning intended. So when it is said 'enjoy yourselves', its meaning is to bring about the meaning of this word, and its well-known meaning is that a contract is made with a woman not intending the purposes of marriage of settling for a child and its upbringing. But for a specified period after which the contract ends, or unspecified meaning the contract remains as long as she is with him until he leaves her. So what is included in it is that which has the root of mut'ah and also time-limited marriage, so it becomes a type of mut'ah, even if contracted with the wording of tazwij and witnesses present.' End quote. Then he gave preponderance in al-Fath to the statement of Zufar regarding the permissibility of time-limited marriage, meaning that it is concluded as permanent and the time condition is void, because the matter is that time-limited marriage is mut'ah, and it is abrogated, but the abrogated is its meaning which the legislation was upon, which is that the contract ends with the end of the period. He does not hold that; rather he says to cancel the condition of time and the contract becomes permanent. His statement: 'He says'; this contract differs from the marriage contract by its termination at the end of the period and by the absence of effects of marriage upon it such as the obligation of mahr, entitlement to inheritance, and other things. His statement: 'And the form of time-limited marriage'; and it does not include the case where one marries her on the condition that he divorces her after a month.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. The author declares that mut'ah (temporary marriage) is not permissible. He explains that it was initially allowed in early Islam, then prohibited, then allowed again, and finally permanently forbidden. The proof is provided through multiple hadith narrated by al-Bukhari and Muslim. The first hadith from Ali states that the Prophet forbade mut'ah at Khaybar in year 7 AH. The second from Samurah mentions permission at the conquest of Makkah in 8 AH followed by prohibition. A third hadith includes the Prophet's statement that Allah has forbidden it until the Day of Judgment. A fourth hadith mentions a temporary permission at Awtas for three days then prohibition. After citing these, the author asserts that no evidence remains for its permissibility. The commentary then quotes al-Fath on the distinction between mut'ah and mu'qqat (time-limited marriage). In mut'ah, the wording uses 'tamattu' (enjoyment), witnesses are not required, and the duration is not necessarily specified. In mu'qqat, the wording uses 'nikah' or 'tazwij', witnesses are required, and the duration is specified. However, the essential characteristic of mut'ah is that the marriage is intended for a limited time, regardless of wording, so mu'qqat is also a form of mut'ah. The commentary then discusses Zufar's view: he considers mu'qqat to be permanent with the time condition void, because the prohibited mut'ah is only that which ends with the period. The Hanafi position, as clarified, is that mu'qqat is also prohibited because it shares the same essence of temporary marriage. The final part notes that the legal effects of such a contract differ from permanent marriage in that there is no obligation of mahr or inheritance.

Key Terms
نكاح المتعة (nikāh al-mutʿah): A marriage contract for a fixed period in exchange for a specified dower, after which the marriage automatically terminates.
المؤقت (al-muʾaqqat): A marriage contract specifying a time duration (e.g., one month) but using the wording of permanent marriage (nikah/tazwij).

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) It is valid but makruh.
D) It is permissible if the husband intends to renew it.

Key Takeaway
Mut'ah marriage and any form of time-limited marriage are permanently prohibited in the Hanafi school.

Nikah — Marriage

Mut'ah, Mu'ajjal, and Conditional Marriages in Hanafi Fiqh
Distinguishes mut'ah from mu'ajjal and rules on conditional marriages

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between mut'ah and mu'ajjal, explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on mu'ajjal, and state the ruling on conditional marriage terms.

Core Idea
Any marriage lacking permanence is effectively mut'ah, but the wording and witnesses distinguish mu'ajjal, leading to a Hanafi internal dispute.

This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya clarifies a critical distinction in marriage contracts: the difference between the abrogated mut'ah and the debated mu'ajjal. It also addresses the validity of conditions in marriage, such as daytime-only companionship. Mastering these distinctions is essential for correctly classifying marriage contracts and understanding the Hanafi internal ikhtilaf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two forms of temporary marriage
Mut'ah uses enjoyment wording (tamattu'), no witnesses required; mu'ajjal uses standard marriage wording (tazwij) with witnesses and specified term.
The form (صورة) is key: mut'ah says 'atamatta' while mu'ajjal says 'tazwij'.
2
Understand the Hanafi majority position
The majority rule mu'ajjal as invalid (batil) because it is a form of mut'ah, which is abrogated. Any marriage with a fixed end lacks the permanence required for valid nikah.
The majority see no substantive difference: both intend a temporary union.
3
Examine Zufar's dissenting view
Zufar holds that mu'ajjal is valid as a permanent marriage; the condition of the term is void, so the marriage becomes permanent and all effects apply.
Zufar's reasoning: the prohibition only applies to contracts that explicitly end with the term, not those that become permanent.
4
Apply the distinction to conditional marriages
A condition like 'I marry you on condition that I divorce you after a month' does not create a temporary marriage; the condition is void and the marriage is permanent because it indicates intent for permanence.
This is not mu'ajjal; it is a valid marriage with an unenforceable condition.
5
Analyze the ruling on daytime marriage
Tazwij al-nahariyat (marrying a woman on condition of daytime-only companionship) is permissible but the condition is not binding; the wife can demand her rights.
Conditions contrary to the essence of marriage are void, but the marriage remains valid.

And the mu'ajjal: The form of mut'ah is that he says 'I enjoy you for such a period for such wealth', and the form of mu'ajjal is that he says 'I married you for such [period] up to a month or ten days.'  11. And it does not mean that the one who does this addresses her with the word 'I enjoy' and the like, because it is known that the word is used and its meaning is intended. So if it is said 'enjoy', its meaning is 'bring about the meaning of this word', and its well-known meaning is that a contract occurs upon a woman that does not intend the purposes of marriage such as stability for the child and his upbringing. (Summarized). Rather [it is] for a specific period, the contract ends with its end, or unspecified meaning that the contract remains as long as he is with her until he leaves her. This includes what is based on the root mut'ah and also mu'ajjal marriage, so it falls under the categories of mut'ah, even if contracted with the wording of tazwij and witnesses are brought.' End. Then in al-Fath he supported the statement of Zufar allowing mu'ajjal, meaning that it is concluded as permanent and the timing is void, because the upshot is that mu'ajjal is mut'ah, which is abrogated. But the abrogated meaning is that which the legislation was upon, i.e., that the contract ends with the expiry of the period, and he (Zufar) does not say that; rather he says to cancel the condition of timing and the contract remains permanent. [2] His statement: 'that he says'; this contract differs from the marriage contract by ending with the end of the period and by the absence of the effects of marriage upon it, such as the obligation of dower, entitlement to inheritance, and others. [3] His statement: 'and the form of mu'ajjal'; it is not included in this that if he marries her on condition that he divorces her after a month, for example, or he intends to stay with her for a specified period, then it is permissible, because the stipulation of a detaching condition indicates that it is concluded as permanent, so the marriage is binding and the condition is void. There is no harm in marrying daytime women, which is that he marries a woman on condition that he is with her during the day only and not at night, and it is appropriate that this condition is not binding.' So in al-Bahr and others.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya, a supercommentary on Sharh al-Wiqaya, addresses the distinction between two types of temporary marriage: mut'ah (pleasure marriage) and mu'ajjal (fixed-term marriage). The author begins by defining the forms: mut'ah uses the wording 'I enjoy' (atamatta') with a specified period and dower, while mu'ajjal uses the standard marriage wording (tazwij) with a fixed term. The commentary from Fath al-Qadir explains that the key difference lies in the wording and the presence of witnesses: mut'ah does not require witnesses, but mu'ajjal does. The historical mut'ah that was permitted and later abrogated is not limited to a specific wording; rather, any marriage that does not intend the permanent purposes of marriage (like procreation and upbringing) is essentially mut'ah. This includes mu'ajjal marriage even if done with proper wording and witnesses. The passage then notes an intra-Hanafi dispute: Imam Zufar holds that a mu'ajjal marriage is valid as a permanent marriage (the condition is void), because the prohibition is only on the type that ends with the term, not on a marriage that becomes permanent. The preferred view in Fath al-Qadir supports Zufar's position. Next, the author clarifies that a conditional marriage (e.g., 'I marry you on condition that I divorce you after a month') is valid and the condition is void, because such a condition indicates that the contract is intended to be permanent, so it becomes a binding marriage. Finally, the ruling on 'daytime marriage' (tazwij al-nahariyat) is discussed: it is permissible for a man to marry a woman with the condition that he stays with her only during the day, but this condition is not binding on him—the wife can later demand her right to nighttime companionship if she has a co-wife. This follows from the principle that conditions contrary to the nature of the marriage contract are void.

Key Terms
متعة (mut'ah): A temporary marriage contracted with the wording 'tamattu' (enjoyment) for a specified dower and period, without witnesses, originally permitted then abrogated.
مؤقت (mu'ajjal): A marriage contracted with standard marriage wording (tazwij) but specifying a fixed term; according to the majority it is invalid, but Zufar holds it valid as permanent.
تزوج النهاريات (tazwij al-nahariyat): Marrying a woman on condition that the husband is with her only during the day, not at night; permissible but the condition is not binding.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on marrying a woman with the condition that he stays with her only during the day?
A) The marriage is invalid because the condition is impermissible.
B) The condition is binding and must be observed.
C) The condition is not binding and the marriage is valid; the wife can demand her rights.
D) The marriage is valid only if the wife agrees to the condition beforehand.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes mut'ah (abrogated) from mu'ajjal (disputed), and conditions contrary to permanence are void.

Nikah — Marriage
beginner
6 min
Ruling on Marrying a Woman with a Daytime-Only Stipulation
Ruling on a marriage condition specifying daytime-only cohabitation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on a marriage condition that limits cohabitation to daytime only, and distinguish between a condition that is valid but non-binding versus invalid.

Core Idea
A marriage condition that does not contradict the essence of marriage is permissible but not legally enforceable.

Marriage contracts often include stipulations about living arrangements. This passage addresses a specific condition: a husband stipulating he will only be with his wife during the day. The Hanafi school clarifies that such a condition does not invalidate the marriage, but it is not binding—meaning the wife cannot enforce it legally. Understanding this distinction is crucial for differentiating between conditions that affect the validity of a contract and those that are merely moral commitments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the condition
Name the act 'tazawwuj al-nahāriyyāt' and describe the condition: the husband will only cohabit during the day.
The condition is a stipulation about timing, not about the duration of the marriage itself.
2
State the ruling
The marriage is valid (sahīh) and the condition is permissible (mubāḥ) but not binding (lāzim).
The phrase 'lā ba'sa' indicates permissibility; 'yanbaghī an lā yakūna lāziman' indicates non-binding.
3
Understand the reasoning
The condition does not contradict the essence of marriage (permanence, mutual rights), so it does not invalidate it. However, it is not considered a binding term because it is not essential to the contract.
Hanafi usul distinguishes between conditions that are part of the contract (binding) and those that are additional promises (non-binding).
4
Note the practical consequence
If the husband breaks the condition, the wife cannot take legal action, but it may be a moral breach.
The wife may have grounds for divorce if the condition was essential to her acceptance, but that is a separate issue.
5
Contrast with other views
Some Shafi'i scholars consider such conditions binding if stipulated, leading to enforceability.
This difference stems from varying usul on the treatment of conditions in contracts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا بأس من تزوج النهاريات، وهو أن يتزوج امرأة على أن يكون عندها نهاراً دون الليل ، وينبغي أن لا يكون هذا الشرط لازماً). كذا في «البحر»(۳) ، وغيره.
And there is no harm in marrying daytime wives, which is that a man marries a woman on the condition that he will be with her during the day and not at night, and it is appropriate that this condition should not be binding. Thus in al-Bahr and others.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage states the ruling on a marriage contract that includes a condition stipulating that the husband will only be with his wife during the day (nahār) and not at night. The author declares that such a condition is permissible (lā ba'sa). However, the phrasing 'it is appropriate that this condition should not be binding' (yanbaghī an lā yakūna hādhā al-sharṭu lāziman) indicates that while the condition is valid as a non-binding promise, it does not become a binding legal stipulation that would affect the contract's enforceability. In other words, the marriage is valid, and the condition is recommended but not obligatory to fulfill. This aligns with the Hanafi position that conditions incompatible with the nature of marriage (such as stipulating a limited duration) are invalid, but conditions that do not contradict the essence of marriage, such as a schedule of visitation, are permissible as a moral commitment but not legally enforceable. The passage references 'al-Bahr' (likely al-Baḥr al-Rā'iq) for further support.

Key Terms
تزوج النهاريات (tazawwuj al-nahāriyyāt): A marriage where the husband stipulates that he will be with his wife during the day only.
لازم (lāzim): Binding; a condition that is legally enforceable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on a condition that the husband be with his wife only during the day?
A) The condition is invalid and the marriage is void.
B) The condition is valid and binding.
C) The marriage is valid but the condition is not binding.
D) The condition is neither valid nor recommended.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a marriage condition that does not contradict the essence of the contract is permissible but not legally enforceable; the marriage remains valid.

Nikah — Marriage

Guardian's Right to Object to a Woman's Self-Marriage
Ruling on the validity of a free woman's marriage without a guardian and the guardian's right to object

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of a free woman's marriage without a guardian and explain the guardian's right to object, including the conditions for objection and its loss after childbirth.

Core Idea
A woman's self-marriage is valid but non-binding; the guardian can annul it for incompatibility until she gives birth.

This lesson examines a distinctive Hanafi ruling: a free, mature woman may contract her own marriage without a guardian's consent, and the marriage is legally valid. However, the guardian retains a right to object on grounds of incompatibility, seeking annulment through a judge. This distinguishes validity from bindingness, a key Hanafi concept. You will explore the conditions for this right and when it ceases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the marriage of a free, legally responsible woman without a guardian. The ruling is that it is nāfidh (valid) but not lāzim (binding).
Nāfidh means legal effects like inheritance and divorce follow.
2
Understand the distinction between validity and bindingness
Validity (ṣiḥḥa) means the contract produces its legal effects; bindingness (luzūm) means it cannot be undone. Here, the marriage is valid but can be annulled by the guardian's objection.
3
Identify the guardian's right and its conditions
The guardian (walī) has the right to object if the woman marries a non-compatible (ghayr kuf') husband. The guardian must be an 'asbah (agnatic heir) by himself.
The objection is for the sake of family honor (ār).
4
Recognize the limitation after childbirth
If the woman gives birth after the marriage, the guardian loses the right to annul, because paternity is established and the perceived disgrace is removed.
Mentioned by Qādī Khān.
5
Distinguish from other schools
The Shafi'i school holds that a guardian is required for the validity of marriage, whereas the Hanafi school holds it is valid but subject to objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نَفَذ نِكَاح حُرَّةٍ مُكَلَّفَةٍ وَلَوْ مِنْ غَيْرِ كُفْءٍ بِلَا وَلِيٍّ، وَلَهُ الِاعْتِرَاضُ هُنَا. باب الولي والكفؤ: الولي لغة: خلاف العدو، والمراد به في مثل هذا المقام: هو الذي ينفذ قوله على الغير شاء أو أبى، ويشترط فيه أن يكون عاقلاً بالغاً. والولاية نوعان: ولاية ندب؛ وهي على المكلفة. وولاية إجبار؛ وهي على الصغير والمعتوهة والمرقوقة. وثبتت ولاية الإجبار بأربع: قرابة، وملك، وولاء، وأمانة. كذا في «الدر المختار وحواشيه». والكفؤ المثل المساوي من المكافأة، بمعنى المساواة، والمراد هنا: المساواة المخصوصة المعتبرة شرعاً. قوله: نفذ؛ المراد بالنفاذ: الصحة المستلزمة لترتب أحكام النكاح؛ كالطلاق والتوارث وغيرهما، لا اللزوم؛ فإن اللازم ما لا يمكن رفعه، وهذا يمكن رفعه باعتراض الولي. قوله: حرة؛ احترز به عن المرقوقة، ولو مكاتبة أو أم ولد، واحترز بالمكلفة عن غير البالغة والمجنونة، فإن نكاحها لا ينفذ بغير ولي. قوله: بلا ولي؛ بلا رضاه وإذنه. قوله: وله الاعتراض؛ دفعاً للعار. عنه، هذا ما لم تلد، فإن ولدت فلا حق للولي في الفسخ، ذكره قاضي خان. قوله: الاعتراض؛ أي لو زوجت نفسها من غير رضى الولي، له أن يعترض فيفسخه القاضي بشرط أن يكون عصبة بنفسه، ولو غير محرم. كذا في «الخانية».
The marriage of a free, legally responsible woman is valid even if not compatible and without a guardian, and he (the guardian) has the right to object here. Chapter of the guardian and compatibility: The guardian linguistically is the opposite of enemy, and what is meant in such a context is the one whose word is binding on others, whether they like it or not, and it is required that he be sane and adult. Guardianship is of two types: guardianship of recommendation (wilāyat nadb), which is over the legally responsible woman; and guardianship of compulsion (wilāyat ijbār), which is over the minor, the insane woman, and the slave woman. And guardianship of compulsion is established by four: kinship, ownership, patronage, and trusteeship. Thus in al-Durr al-Mukhtār and its glosses. And al-kufu' is the like, the equal, from mukāfa'ah, meaning equality, and what is intended here is the specific equality recognized by law. His statement: 'nafadha' means validity that entails the consequences of marriage, such as divorce, inheritance, etc., not binding (luzūm); for binding is that which cannot be lifted, and this can be lifted by the guardian's objection. His statement: 'free' excludes the slave woman, even if she is a mukātabah or umm walad; and his statement 'legally responsible' excludes the minor and the insane, for their marriage is not valid without a guardian. His statement: 'without a guardian' means without his consent and permission. His statement: 'and he has the right to object' is to ward off disgrace. This is so as long as she has not given birth; if she has given birth, then the guardian has no right to annulment; mentioned by Qādī Khān. His statement: 'objection' means if she married herself without the guardian's approval, he may object and the judge will annul it on condition that he be an inheriting agnate ('asbah) by himself, even if not a mahram. Thus in al-Khāniyyah.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on guardianship and compatibility. It presents the foundational Hanafi ruling that a free, mature woman (ḥurrah mukallafah) has the right to conclude her own marriage contract without a guardian's consent, and her marriage is valid (nāfidh) even if she marries a man who is not her equal (kuf') in status. However, the text immediately qualifies this with 'and he (the guardian) has the right to object here,' meaning that the marriage, though valid, is not binding (laysa bi lāzim) because the guardian can challenge it. The commentary explains that nāfidh here means the marriage produces legal effects like inheritance and divorce, but it is subject to annulment by the guardian's objection. The guardian's right exists only if he is an 'asbah (agnatic relative) by himself, i.e., a close male relative. This right is lost if the woman gives birth because the paternity is established and the 'disgrace' (ār) is removed. The passage then defines guardianship types: wilāyat nadb (non-compulsory guardianship for a competent woman) and wilāyat ijbār (compulsory guardianship over minors, insane, and slaves). The basis of compulsory guardianship is four: kinship, ownership (of a slave), patronage (walā'), and trusteeship. The last part defines kuf' (compatibility) as equality in specific legal criteria like lineage, religion, profession, etc., but the details are elaborated elsewhere. This ruling illustrates the Hanafi position that a woman's capacity to marry is independent, but the guardian has a protective right based on family honor (ār).

Key Terms
ولي (walī): A guardian who has authority over the marriage of the woman.
كفؤ (kuf'): Compatibility in marriage, meaning equality in lineage, religion, profession, etc.
ولاية ندب (wilāyat nadb): Guardianship of recommendation, where the guardian's consent is recommended but not required for a mature woman.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the ruling say about a free, mature woman marrying without a guardian?
A) The marriage is invalid.
B) The marriage is valid but the guardian can object.
C) The marriage is valid and binding with no right of objection.
D) The marriage requires the guardian's consent for validity.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that a free, mature woman can marry without a guardian; the marriage is valid but the guardian can object for incompatibility until she gives birth.
